The Proof of Contingency from the World
The poet presents the proof of contingency by observing the world and concluding the necessity of the Eternal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the observation of the world leads to the proof of contingency and the necessity of an Eternal Creator.

Core Idea
The world's contingency proves it cannot be eternal — it must have a Creator.

This lesson presents the classic kalam proof for God's existence based on contingency. The poet guides us to observe the world — from ourselves to the heavens to the earth — and recognize the mark of creation. This very creation reveals that the world is contingent, meaning it could have not existed, and therefore cannot be eternal. This makes the existence of an Eternal Creator necessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage in Arabic and English
Begin by reading the complete poetic argument in Arabic and its literal translation. Familiarize yourself with the flow of the proof without overanalyzing.
2
Identify the three realms of observation
The poet instructs you to look at yourself, then the upper world (celestial), then the lower world (terrestrial). Notice what all three share: a wondrous act of wisdom (ṣun' badi' al-ḥikam).
This observation is the starting point of the proof.
3
Understand the logical inference
The wondrous creation establishes the proof of contingency (dalīl al-‘adam). Anything for which non-existence is possible cannot be eternal (qadīm). Therefore, the world is originated (ḥadīth) and requires an Eternal Creator.
The poem concludes that eternity is impossible for contingent things.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فانظر إلى نفسك ثم انتقل للعالم العلوي ثم السفلي تجد به صنعا بديع الحكم لكن به قام دليل العدم وكل ما جاز عليه العدم عليه قطعا يستحيل القدم
So look at yourself, then move to the upper world, then the lower world; you will find in it a wondrous act of wisdom, but through it the proof of contingency (al-'adam) is established. And everything that contingency (al-'adam) is possible for, upon it certainly the attribute of eternity (al-qidam) is impossible.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage presents the classic kalam proof for the existence of God based on contingency (huduth). The poet instructs the reader to first observe himself, then the celestial (upper) world, then the terrestrial (lower) world. In all of this, one finds a wondrous creation (ṣun' badi' al-hikam). However, this very creation establishes the evidence (dalil) of contingency (al-'adam, literally 'non-existence' but here meaning possibility of non-existence/change). The key inference: anything that is subject to possibility of non-existence cannot be eternal (qadim); it must be originated (hadith). Thus, the world is originated and requires an Eternal Creator.

Key Terms
نفس
عالم علوي
عالم سفلي
صنع
دليل العدم
جاز عليه العدم
يستحيل القدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think 'dālīl al-‘adam' means a proof that God does not exist.
Correction: It actually means proof of the world's contingency — that the world could have not existed, not that God does not exist.

Checkpoint
What does the poet say about everything that is subject to possible non-existence?
A) It is eternal
B) It is impossible to be eternal
C) It is necessary
D) It is possible

Key Takeaway
By observing the wondrous design in ourselves and the world, we realize the world is contingent and cannot be eternal, pointing to the necessity of an Eternal Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are commanded to look at yourself first. When you honestly examine your own existence, do you truly live as if you are contingent, dependent on God every moment, or do you behave as if you are self-sufficient? What would change if you internalized that you could cease to exist at any instant?
?
The poet says you will find a 'wondrous act of wisdom' in the world. Have you ever paused to see the wisdom in your own body's design? If not, what does that say about your heedlessness? Will you commit to a daily practice of reflection on God's signs in yourself?
?
This passage states that whatever contingency is possible for cannot be eternal. Your soul is contingent. Are you investing in things that are also contingent (wealth, status, relationships) while neglecting the Eternal? What is one tangible step you can take today to prioritize the Eternal over the temporary?

Necessary Attributes of God: Existence, Eternality, Self-Subsistence, Oneness
Poem listing necessary attributes of God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rationally necessary attributes of God as enumerated by Imam al-Laqqani, including existence, eternality, self-subsistence, and oneness.

Core Idea
God's necessary attributes are rationally required, not merely derived from revelation.

This passage from Jawharat al-Tawhid lists the essential attributes that reason itself demands for the Creator. Before exploring any theological arguments, the poet establishes these foundational qualities that every Muslim theologian affirms. As you read, notice how each attribute is presented as a necessary consequence of what God must be.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the attributes
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. List the six attributes mentioned by the poet.
The attributes are: existence, pre-eternity, perpetuity, dissimilarity, self-subsistence, oneness.
2
Understand the rational necessity of each attribute
For each attribute, ask: why must God have this? For example, without existence He would be nothing; without pre-eternity something else would precede Him.
The poet links dissimilarity to pre-eternity as a proof.
3
Connect to the broader theological tradition
Reflect that these attributes are universally affirmed in Sunni theology and are the basis for further discussions about God's actions and names.
This passage is a concise summary of the necessary attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فواجب له الوجود والقدم كذا بقاء لا يشاب بالعدم. وأنه لما ينال العدم مخالف برهان هذا القدم. قيامه بالنفس وحدانية منزهة أوصافه سنية.
Necessary for Him are existence and pre-eternity, likewise perpetuity not mixed with non-existence. And that He is unlike that which attains non-existence—the proof of this pre-eternity. His self-subsistence, oneness, exalted are His attributes, lofty.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage enumerates the necessary attributes (sifat wajiba) of God. First: wujud (existence) and qidam (pre-eternity) and baqa (perpetuity) without any possibility of non-existence. Second: He is mukhalaf (different) from anything that can cease, which is a proof of His qidam. Third: qiyam bi al-nafs (self-subsistence), wahdaniyya (oneness), and that His attributes are munazzaha (transcendent) and saniyya (lofty).

Key Terms
الوجود
القدم
البقاء
المخالفة
القيام بالنفس
الوحدانية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The necessary attributes of God are only known from revelation and not from reason.
Correction: The poem shows that these attributes are rationally necessary, not merely scriptural. Reason itself demands that God must have these attributes.

Checkpoint
Which attribute is described as 'قيامه بالنفس'?
A) Self-subsistence
B) Oneness
C) Eternity
D) Power

Key Takeaway
God must have existence, eternality, perpetuity, dissimilarity to creation, self-subsistence, and oneness – all rationally necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's existence is rationally necessary, why do you sometimes act as if He might not be watching? What would it take for you to live with the same certainty of His existence as you have of your own breathing?
?
God is self-subsistent; you depend on Him every moment. When was the last time you truly felt that dependence in your heart, not just in your mind? What practical change can you make today to remind yourself of that need?
?
The poem says God's attributes are 'lofty' and 'transcendent.' Do you habitually think of God in human-like terms, and if so, how can you begin to purify your conception of Him starting now? What specific thought pattern will you repent from?

Why Tawḥīd Is the Noblest Teaching
Author answers why tawḥīd is specifically mentioned.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author singles out tawḥīd for mention among the many teachings of the Prophet, and why it is considered the noblest act of worship.

Core Idea
Tawḥīd is the highest and most noble act of worship.

In this short passage, the author anticipates a reader's question: if the Prophet brought many teachings, why does the poem focus only on tawḥīd? The answer reveals a key principle in Islamic theology: tawḥīd is not just one teaching among equals—it is the most excellent form of worship. Let's see how the author defends this claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Start by reading the objection in the passage: someone asks why tawḥīd is singled out when the Prophet brought many other things. Notice the structure: 'فإن قلت ... فالجواب' signals a typical question-and-answer format in Islamic texts.
This is a common rhetorical device to address expected doubts.
2
Identify the core answer
The author's answer is based on the principle that tawḥīd is the noblest of acts of worship (أشرف العبادات). Understand that 'ashraf' means highest in rank and merit. The author also mentions that prayer comes next in nobility.
The phrase 'ويليه الصلاة' shows the hierarchy of worship.
3
Examine the supporting hadith
The author supports the answer with a hadith from Abī Sa‘īd that Allah did not prescribe anything better than tawḥīd and prayer. This hadith reinforces the superiority of tawḥīd. Reflect on how scriptural evidence is used to justify the claim.
The hadith is cited to ground the answer in revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت : فلماذا خصَّ التوحيد بالذكر ، مع أنه الله جاء بأشياء كثيرة غير التوحيد ؟ فالجواب: أنَّه إنما خَصَّ التوحيد بالذَّكرِ مِن بين سائر ما جاء به النَّبيُّ ؛ لأنَّ التَّوحيد أشرف العبادات ، ويليه الصَّلاة، كما قد جاء في حديث أبي سعيد: إنّ الله تعالى لم يفرض شيئًا أفضل من التَّوحيدِ والصلاة.
For if you say: Why did he single out tawḥīd for mention, when the Prophet came with many things other than tawḥīd? The answer: He only singled out tawḥīd for mention among all that the Prophet brought, because tawḥīd is the noblest of acts of worship, followed by prayer, as has come in the ḥadīth of Abī Sa‘īd: 'Indeed Allah the Exalted did not prescribe anything better than tawḥīd and prayer.'
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage presents an objection-and-answer format. The objector asks why the poem's line 'alā nabiyyin jā'a bi-l-tawḥīd' specifies tawḥīd when the Prophet brought many other teachings such as prayer, fasting, etc. The answer is that tawḥīd is singled out because it is the noblest (ashraf) of acts of worship, and prayer comes next in rank. The author supports this with a ḥadīth from Abī Sa‘īd al-Khudrī (may Allah be pleased with him) that Allah did not prescribe anything better than tawḥīd and prayer. This shows the superiority of tawḥīd over all other obligations, justifying its specific mention.

Key Terms
التوحيد
الصلاة
أشرف العبادات
حديث أبي سعيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think tawḥīd is just one of many equal teachings; this shows it is the highest.
Correction: Tawḥīd is not equal to other acts of worship; it is the most noble and excellent, as explicitly stated by the author and supported by hadith.

Checkpoint
Why is tawḥīd singled out for mention according to the passage?
A) Because it is the most difficult teaching to understand
B) Because it is the noblest act of worship
C) Because the Prophet only brought tawḥīd initially
D) Because other teachings are not important

Key Takeaway
Tawḥīd is the noblest of all acts of worship, which is why the poem specifically highlights it among the Prophet's many teachings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If tawḥīd is the noblest act of worship, does your heart truly prioritize knowing and affirming Allah's oneness over all other duties? Or do you give more attention to outward rituals while neglecting the foundation?
?
Examine your daily life: when you engage in acts of worship, do you consciously connect them to tawḥīd, or do you perform them out of habit? What would change if you made tawḥīd the constant focus of every act?
?
The hadith says Allah 'did not prescribe anything better than tawḥīd and prayer.' Are you treating your prayer as a mere routine, or as a living expression of your tawḥīd? What step will you take today to renew the sincerity of your prayer?

Why Tawhid is singled out among all teachings
Answer to why Tawhid is specifically mentioned

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Prophet's message is specifically identified with Tawhid, even though he brought many other teachings.

Core Idea
Tawhid is the noblest worship and the key to all other acts.

This lesson addresses a common question: if the Prophet brought many commands, why is his mission so closely tied to Tawhid? The answer reveals that Tawhid is not just one teaching among equals—it is the foundation that gives all other acts their value and validity.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the passage carefully. Notice the question raised and how the author responds with multiple reasons.
The passage follows an objection-and-answer format common in Islamic scholarly works.
2
Identify the two main reasons
The answer gives two key points: (1) Tawhid is the noblest act of worship, and (2) it is a condition for validity of other acts and a cause for salvation.
The hadith of Abu Sa'id is cited to support the nobility of Tawhid and prayer.
3
Reflect on the implications
Think about why Tawhid is considered the foundation. How does this affect the importance of other acts?
This connects to the broader theme of Tawhid as the core of Islamic faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت : فلماذا خصَّ التوحيد بالذكر ، مع أنه الله جاء بأشياء كثيرة غير التوحيد ؟ فالجواب: أنَّه إنما خَصَّ التوحيد بالذَّكرِ مِن بين سائر ما جاء به النَّبيُّ ؛ لأنَّ التَّوحيد أشرف العبادات ، ويليه الصَّلاة، كما قد جاء في حديث أبي سعيد: إنّ الله تعالى لم يفرض شيئًا أفضل من التَّوحيدِ والصلاة. وقد أشار الشارع إلى ذلك السؤال والجواب. محي. وتخصيص الإرسال بالتَّوحيد ؛ لأنَّه أشرف العبادات، وأفضل الطَّاعات، وشرطٌ في صحتها ، وسبب في النَّجَاة من العذاب المُخلد
If you say: Why was Tawhid specifically mentioned, when Allah came with many things other than Tawhid? The answer: He only specifically mentioned Tawhid among all that the Prophet brought because Tawhid is the noblest of acts of worship, followed by prayer, as it came in the hadith of Abu Sa'id: Indeed Allah did not prescribe anything better than Tawhid and prayer. And the commentator indicated this question and answer. Mohyi. And the specification of the sending with Tawhid because it is the noblest of acts of worship, the best of acts of obedience, a condition for their validity, and a cause for salvation from eternal punishment.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage addresses a potential objection: since the Prophet brought many teachings, why is Tawhid specifically mentioned? The answer is that Tawhid is the noblest of acts of worship, followed by prayer, as supported by a hadith. The commentator then reaffirms that the mission was specifically with Tawhid because it is the best of acts of obedience, a condition for their validity, and a cause for salvation from eternal punishment. The structure is an objection-and-answer followed by a reinforcing statement.

Key Terms
التوحيد
أشرف العبادات
شرط في الصحة
النجاة من العذاب المخلد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All acts of worship are equally important, and Tawhid is just one among many.
Correction: Tawhid is the noblest act of worship, a condition for validity of other acts, and the cause for salvation from eternal punishment, as stated in the passage.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why was Tawhid specifically mentioned?
A) Because it is the most difficult
B) Because it is the noblest act of worship
C) Because it is the only command
D) Because it was new

Key Takeaway
Tawhid is the noblest act of worship, a condition for the validity of other acts, and the key to salvation from eternal punishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you consciously prioritize strengthening your Tawhid over other good deeds, or do you find yourself more focused on actions like prayer and charity? What would change if you truly believed Tawhid is the noblest worship?
?
Think of a time you performed a good deed. Did you check whether your intention was purely for Allah, grounded in Tawhid, or did you assume the deed would be accepted automatically? How might this lesson change your approach?
?
If you were to die today, would you be confident that your Tawhid is sound enough to be the cause of your salvation from eternal punishment? If not, what specific steps will you take starting now to strengthen it?

Why Only Allah is Called 'al-Haqq' (the Truth)
Commentary explains why only Allah is called 'al-Haqq' (the Truth).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why only Allah deserves the attribute 'al-Haqq' (the Truth) and how His necessary existence by His own essence distinguishes Him from all created beings.

Core Idea
Only Allah is al-Haqq because His existence is necessary, eternal, and uncaused.

The phrase 'Din al-Haqq' in the poem is interpreted by the commentator as pointing to Allah Himself, the ultimate Reality. This lesson unpacks the exclusive divine name 'al-Haqq' and the reason for its exclusivity: Allah's existence is by His own essence, with no beginning or end.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice how the commentator defines 'al-Haqq' as the one whose existence is realized and established.
2
Identify the Claim
The commentator states that none other than Allah deserves the description 'al-Haqq'. This is the main claim to understand.
Why is this claim being made? Look for the reason.
3
Understand the Reason
The reason given is that Allah's existence is by His own essence (li-dhātihī). This means His existence is necessary, not contingent on anything else.
Also note: no non-existence precedes or follows Him.
4
Contrast with Creation
All other beings are contingent – they come into existence after non-existence and may cease to exist. Therefore, they cannot be called 'al-Haqq' in the absolute sense.
This contrast highlights the uniqueness of Allah.
5
Apply to 'Din al-Haqq'
Now understand that 'Din al-Haqq' is the religion that leads to and is based on this ultimate Truth, Allah. It is not just a name for a religion but a description of its source.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَدَلَّهُمْ لِدِينِ الحَقِّ أَيْ عَلَى دِينِ الحَقِّ المُتَحَقِّقِ وَالثَّابِتِ وُجُودُهُ وَهُوَ اللَّهُ تَعَالَى وَلَا يَسْتَحِقُّ هَذَا الوَصْفَ غَيْرُهُ لِأَنَّ وُجُودَهُ لِذَاتِهِ لَا يَسْبِقُهُ عَدَمٌ وَلَا يَلْحَقُهُ عَدَمٌ
And he guided them to the religion of truth (al-ḥaqq), that is, to the religion of truth (al-ḥaqq), the realized and established one whose existence is, and He is Allah the Exalted. None other deserves this description because His existence is by His own essence, no non-existence precedes it and no non-existence follows it.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage is a definition and affirmation concerning the phrase 'the religion of truth' (dīn al-ḥaqq) in the poetic line. The commentator explains that 'al-ḥaqq' here refers to the one who is truly existent and fixed in being, namely Allah. He then argues that no other being deserves this attribute because Allah's existence is by His own essence (li-dhātihī) — it is necessary, eternal, and uncaused. This means that nothing preceded His existence (no prior non-existence) and nothing will annihilate Him (no subsequent non-existence). All other beings are contingent and preceded by non-existence, so they cannot be described as 'the truth' in this absolute sense.

Key Terms
الحَقّ
وُجُودِهِ لِذَاتِهِ
دِين الحَقّ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think 'dīn al-ḥaqq' refers solely to the name of a religion, without realizing it refers to Allah Himself as the ultimate truth.
Correction: The commentator explains that 'al-Haqq' here describes Allah, because only He has necessary existence by His own essence. Thus 'Dīn al-Haqq' is the religion that leads to Allah, the Truth.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator say no one besides Allah deserves the description 'al-ḥaqq'?
A) Because Allah named Himself al-Ḥaqq in the Quran.
B) Because only Allah has necessary existence by His own essence, without being preceded or followed by non-existence.
C) Because other religions are false.
D) Because the word 'ḥaqq' can only apply to God.

Key Takeaway
Only Allah deserves the name 'al-Haqq' because His existence is necessary, eternal, and uncaused, whereas all created beings are contingent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that only Allah's existence is without beginning or end. Everything else you rely on—health, relationships, wealth—is contingent and will pass. What is one thing you are holding onto as if it will last forever, and how will you begin to detach your heart from it today?
?
If Allah is the only true reality (al-Haqq) and your own existence is fleeting, what does that mean for your fears of failure or loss? Choose one fear you have been carrying and consciously hand it over to the One whose existence is never touched by non-existence.
?
The source says Allah's existence is by His essence, not caused by anything. Are there areas in your life where you act as if your identity or worth depends on created things? Name one dependency (like approval, success, or comfort) that you need to re-anchor in Allah as the sole source of stability.

Condition of the Call: Who is Accountable?
Sanusi explains the condition of da'wa reaching a person and the two opinions on which prophet's call suffices.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the condition that the da'wa must reach a person for them to be a mukallaf, and the two scholarly opinions on which prophet's call is required for accountability.

Core Idea
The call must reach you; but did it need to be from your own prophet?

This passage from Sanusi addresses a critical question: are people accountable if they never received a prophetic call? The answer is nuanced, involving the concept of 'ahl al-fatrah' and a key verse from the Quran. Let's walk through the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the English translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage begins with an exception and then presents two opinions.
2
Identify the two opinions
Find where Sanusi describes the two views on which prophet's call is required. Mark the first opinion (any messenger) and the second (the messenger sent to their time).
The second opinion is attributed to 'ahl al-tahqeeq' (the investigators).
3
Understand ahl al-fatrah
Focus on the definition of ahl al-fatrah and the example of the ancient Arabs at the time of Jesus. Ask yourself why they are saved despite idol worship.
The evidence from Quran 17:15 is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخرج بمن بلغته الدعوة: مَنْ لم تبلغه ، وذلك بأن نشأ في شاهق جبل ; فليس هذا بمكلف على الأصح، وقيل: هو مكلف لوجود العقل الكافي في وجوب المعرفة، وإن لم تبلغه الدعوة، والذين اشترطوا بلوغ دعوة الرسول يختلفون في أنه: هل الشَّرط أن تبلغه دعوة رسول أي رسول كان ، أم الشَّرط أن تبلغه دعوة الرسول الذي أُرسل إلى أهل زمانه ؟ وذهب جماعة إلى الأوَّل، وذهب جماعة إلى الثَّاني ، وهو مذهب أهل التحقيق وعليه يكون أهل الفترة - وهم الذين كانوا بين أزمنة الرُّسل - ناجين وإن بدلوا وغيَّروا ، وعبدوا الأصنام، والدليل على ذلك قوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَا كُنَّا مُعَذِّبِينَ حَتَّى نَبْعَثَ رَسُولًا ﴾ [الإسراء: ١٥] (۱) قوله: (الذي بلغته الدعوة ولا بد على التحقيق من أن يكون الرسول لهم كما نقله الملوي عن الأبي في شرح مسلم خلافا للنووي فالعرب القدماء الذين أدركوا عيسى من أهل الفترة على المُعتمد لأنه لم يرسل لهم ، وإنما أرسل لبني إسرائيل، وكذا يعطي حكم أهل الفترة من بني إسرائيل من لم يدرك نبينا ونشأ بعد تغيير الإنجيل بحيث لم يبلغه الشرع الصحيح، لا إن بلغه ولو بعد رفع عيسى ، بناء على أن شرع الأنبياء السابقين لا ينسخ إلا بمجيء نبي آخر لا بمجرد الموت
And by 'one whom the call has reached' is excluded: one whom it has not reached, such as one who grew up on a mountain peak; this one is not a mukallaf according to the most correct (opinion), and it is said: he is a mukallaf because of the existence of sufficient intellect for the obligation of knowledge, even if the call has not reached him. And those who stipulate the arrival of the call of a messenger differ: is the condition that the call of any messenger reach him, or that the call of the messenger sent to the people of his time reach him? A group adopted the first, and a group adopted the second—this is the doctrine of the investigators, and accordingly the People of the Interval (ahl al-fatrah)—those who lived between the times of messengers—are saved even if they changed and altered and worshipped idols. The evidence for that is His statement the Exalted: 'And We were not to punish until We sent a messenger' (al-Isra: 15). (1) His statement: 'One whom the call has reached, and it is necessary on the investigation that the messenger be for them, as al-Mallawi transmitted from al-Abi in Sharh Muslim, contrary to al-Nawawi. So the ancient Arabs who encountered Jesus are from ahl al-fatrah according to the relied-upon (view) because he was not sent to them; rather, he was sent to the Children of Israel. Similarly, from the Children of Israel, the one who did not meet our Prophet and grew up after the alteration of the Gospel such that the correct law did not reach him is given the ruling of ahl al-fatrah, not if it reached him even after the raising of Jesus, based on the fact that the law of previous prophets is only abrogated by the arrival of another prophet, not by mere death.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage explains the condition that the call (da'wa) must reach a person for them to be a mukallaf. It presents the case of someone who grew up isolated on a mountain: the correct opinion is that such a person is not a mukallaf, though some argue that the intellect alone is sufficient for obligation. The passage then focuses on those who require the arrival of a prophet's call: there are two views—(a) any prophet's call suffices, or (b) only the prophet specifically sent to their time. The investigators adopt the second, meaning ahl al-fatrah are saved even if they committed shirk. Evidence is the Quranic verse that Allah does not punish until He sends a messenger. The footnote elaborates: for the call to be effective, the messenger must have been sent to that particular people. Thus, Arabs contemporary with Jesus are from ahl al-fatrah because Jesus was sent only to Israel. Similarly, Israelites who lived after the alteration of the Gospel and never received the correct law are like ahl al-fatrah, unless they actually received it. This is because previous prophets' laws are abrogated only by a new prophet, not by death.

مكلف
أهل الفترة
بلوغ الدعوة
رسول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A person is a mukallaf simply because they have intellect, even without a prophetic call.
Correction: The correct opinion is that the call must reach them; isolated individuals are not mukallaf (though a minority holds the opposite).

Checkpoint
According to the investigators, what is the status of the ancient Arabs who lived at the time of Jesus?
A) They are mukallaf because they had intellect.
B) They are from ahl al-fatrah because Jesus was sent only to Israel.
C) They are punished for idolatry.
D) They are bound by the law of Moses.

Key Takeaway
For accountability, the call of a messenger sent specifically to your people must reach you; otherwise, you are like ahl al-fatrah and not punished.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have received the call of Prophet Muhammad ﷺ. Have you ever truly considered the weight of that accountability? What would you say if asked tomorrow what you have done with that knowledge?
?
The ahl al-fatrah were saved despite their idolatry because no messenger reached them. You are not among them. Are you living as though you are? What specific aspect of your life today would change if you fully believed that you will be asked about every message you received?
?
If someone today hears of Islam but only through distorted media, are they still accountable? This lesson suggests they might be excused if the true message didn't reach them. Now think: who in your circle has not truly received the message? What is your responsibility to convey it clearly, even without speaking?

Three Schools on the Obligation of Knowing God
Sanusi enumerates three schools on knowledge of God and their relation to reason and revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the positions of the Ash'aris, Maturidis, and Mu'tazila on whether knowledge of God is obligatory by reason or by revelation, and the key differences between the latter two schools.

Core Idea
Reason or revelation? Three schools answer differently, shaping Islamic theology.

Islamic theologians debated whether humans are obliged to know God before revelation arrives. This passage from a classical commentary outlines the three main positions: Ash'aris, Maturidis, and Mu'tazila. Understanding these distinctions clarifies the broader role of reason in faith.

Guided Path
1
Understand the meaning of 'شرعاً'
The passage begins by explaining the poem's phrase 'شرعاً' (by the Law). Note the grammatical construction and its implication: obligation to know God is only through revelation.
2
Learn the three schools' positions
Read the three summaries: Ash'aris say all rulings from revelation; Maturidis say only the obligation to know God is from reason; Mu'tazila say all rulings are from reason.
Focus on the scope of reason in each school.
3
Identify the two differences between Maturidis and Mu'tazila
The passage presents two key distinctions: scope (knowledge only vs. all rulings) and the basis of reason's judgment (self-evident vs. moral judgment).
These differences are often misunderstood.
4
Grasp the refutation implied by 'شرعاً'
The phrase 'شرعاً' refutes the Mu'tazila and Maturidis, showing the Ash'ari position that obligation comes solely from revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله: (شرعاً) منصوب بنزع الخافض أي بالشرع، متعلق بـ (وجبا عليه) لكنه قدمه لإفادة الحصر، والمعنى: لا يجب على المكلف أن يعرف ما قد وجبا لله عقلا إلا بالشرع؛ إذ قبله لا حكم. ثم شرح المذاهب الثلاثة: الأول مذهب الأشاعرة، وحاصله: أن جميع الأحكام ومنها معرفة الله تعالى إنما تثبت بالشرع، ويكلف بها العقلاء. والثاني مذهب أبي منصور الماتريدي، وحاصله: أن المعرفة وحدها تثبت بالعقل، وأما سائر الأحكام فلا تثبت إلا بالشرع. والثالث مذهب المعتزلة، وحاصله: أن الأحكام كلها ـ ومنها معرفة الله تعالى ـ ثبتت بالعقل، وجاء الشرع مبينا ومؤكدا لما أثبته العقل. ومما قررناه لك في بيان هذه المذاهب الثلاثة تفهم أن الفرق بين مذهب الماتريدية والمعتزلة من جهتين: الأولى: أن الماتريدية خصوا ما أثبته العقل بالمعرفة، وأما المعتزلة فجعلوا ما يثبته العقل عاما في كل الأحكام. الثانية: أن معنى إثبات العقل لوجوب المعرفة عند الماتريدية أنه يستطيع حسن الاستقلال بإدراكه لكونه واضحا جلياً، ومعنى ذلك عند المعتزلة أنه رأى أن وجوب المعرفة حسن فحكم بثبوته، وفرق بين المعنيين. وإذا علمت هذا كله تبين لك أن قول المصنف والشارح شرعاً رد على طائفتين من المتكلمين، وهما المعتزلة والماتريدية.
And his statement: (by law) is in the accusative by removing the preposition, i.e., by the revealed law, attached to 'became obligatory upon him' but he advanced it to indicate restriction, and the meaning: it is not obligatory upon the legally responsible person to know what is obligatory for Allah rationally except through the revealed law; since before it there is no ruling. Then he explained the three schools: the first is the school of the Ash'aris, its summary: that all rulings including knowledge of Allah Most High are established only by the revealed law, and the rational beings are obligated by it. The second is the school of Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, its summary: that knowledge alone is established by reason, but other rulings are established only by the revealed law. The third is the school of the Mu'tazila, its summary: that all rulings including knowledge of Allah Most High are established by reason, and the revealed law came clarifying and confirming what reason established. And from what we have presented to you in the explanation of these three schools, you understand that the difference between the Maturidi school and the Mu'tazila is from two aspects: the first: that the Maturidis specified what reason establishes to knowledge, while the Mu'tazila made what reason establishes general to all rulings. The second: that the meaning of reason's establishment of the obligation of knowledge according to the Maturidis is that it can independently perceive it because it is clear and evident, and its meaning according to the Mu'tazila is that it saw that the obligation of knowledge is good and thus judged its establishment; and there is a difference between the two meanings. When you know all this, it becomes clear to you that the statement of the author and the commentator 'by law' is a refutation of two groups of theologians, namely the Mu'tazila and the Maturidis.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage is a classification and explanation of the three schools regarding the obligation of knowledge of God. It begins by explaining the word 'شرعاً' (by law) from the poem: it is accusative due to removing a preposition, meaning 'by the revealed law', and it is attached to 'وجبا' (obligatory). The phrase 'إلا بالشرع' (except through the law) indicates that before revelation, there is no ruling—this is the Ash'ari position. The author then outlines three schools: 
1. Ash'aris: All rulings, including knowledge of God, are established only by revelation; reason has no role in obligation. 
2. Maturidis: Only the obligation to know God is established by reason; all other rulings come from revelation. 
3. Mu'tazila: All rulings are established by reason; revelation merely confirms. 
The passage then explains two key differences between Maturidis and Mu'tazila: 
- Scope: Maturidis limit reason's role to knowledge of God; Mu'tazila extend it to all rulings. 
- Mode: For Maturidis, reason independently perceives the obligation because it is self-evident; for Mu'tazila, reason judges it as good and thus necessary. 
The conclusion is that the phrase 'شرعاً' in the poem refutes both the Mu'tazila (who deny that obligation comes only from revelation) and the Maturidis (who say knowledge alone is from reason).

المعرفة
الشرع
العقل

الماتريدية
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidis and Mu'tazila are identical because both use reason.
Correction: They differ in scope (knowledge only vs. all rulings) and the basis of reason's judgment (self-evident vs. moral inference), as explained in the passage.

Checkpoint

B) Maturidis
C) Mu'tazila
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
The three schools differ fundamentally on whether reason or revelation establishes the obligation to know God, with the Ash'ari position that only revelation obligates being the basis of the poem's phrase 'شرعاً'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you rely more on your own reasoning or on Allah's revealed law to determine what is obligatory for you? Read the passage again: the Ash'aris say all obligations come only through revelation. Have you ever acted on a 'feeling' of obligation without checking the Shari'ah? Will you now commit to seeking revelation-based answers before assuming something is required?
?
The Maturidis claim that reason can independently perceive the obligation to know God. Do you think you could have recognized your duty to know Allah without Islamic teaching? How has your actual journey to knowledge of Allah involved both reason and revelation, and what does that say about you?
?
If the Ash'ari position is correct, then you are not blameworthy for what you did not know before revelation reached you. But after knowing this lesson, you now carry a greater responsibility. Are you actively seeking knowledge of Allah from the revealed sources, or are you content with minimal effort? Will you change your daily routine today to prioritize learning your obligations?

Ash'ari: Knowledge First, Nazar Obligatory as Means
Al-Bajuri presents the Ash'ari view and the proof that nazar is obligatory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari argument that knowledge of Allah is the first obligation and that reasoning (nazar) becomes obligatory as a necessary means to attain that knowledge.

Core Idea
Knowledge of Allah is the first duty; reasoning is its necessary means.

Al-Bajuri presents the well-known Ash'ari position that the very first thing required of a responsible person is knowledge of Allah. But this knowledge is not obtained without engaged reasoning (nazar). In this passage, he lays out the logical proof that makes nazar an obligation, and also clarifies which disagreements are worth considering.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation to get the gist of the argument.
Notice the phrase 'لتوقفها عليه' which indicates dependence.
2
Identify the two main claims
The passage makes two major points: (1) knowledge is first; nazar is obligatory as its means. (2) Only disagreements with a rational basis are considered.
The text explicitly says 'والأمر الثاني' to mark the second point.
3
Understand the syllogism proof
The marginal note explains the logical form: knowledge is obligatory, nazar is a means to it, so nazar is obligatory. This is a first-figure syllogism.
The syllogism is: (Major) Everything upon which an obligatory depends is itself obligatory. (Minor) Nazar is that upon which the obligatory knowledge depends. Therefore, nazar is obligatory.
4
Reflect on the significance
This establishes nazar as an individual obligation for the mukallaf, not just a recommendation. It also sets the foundation for theological inquiry.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمشهور عن الأشعري (۱) إمام أهلِ السُّنَّة الذي بُنيت هذه المنظومة على مختارِه.. أنَّ المعرفة أوَّلُ وَاجب على المكلَّف؛ لأنَّ جميع الواجبات لا تتحقق إلا بها (۲)، فَاجْزم اعتقادك به، به ، واختَرْهُ غيرَ مُلتفت إلى غيره لأرجحيَّته، لكنَّه لا يتوصل إليها إلَّا بالنَّظر؛ فهو واجب بوجوبها ؛ لتوقفها عليه، مع كونه مقدورًا للمُكلَّف (۳) ، وكلُّ ما هو كذلك .. = قيمة هذا القول . والأمر الثاني: أنه لم يعتد بخلاف من خالف في هذين الأمرين ؛ لأنه ليس كلُّ خلاف يُؤْبَه له ، إنما يؤبه بالخلاف الذي له وجه من النظر. محي.

قوله : ( لتوقفها عليه ) أي لتوقف المعرفة على النظر . وقوله: (مع كونه) أي: النظر مقدورا للمكلف، وأنت خبير بأنه قوله : (التوقفها عليه ... الخ ) متضمن لقياس من الشكل الأول، تقديره : النظر موقوف عليه المعرفة الوجبة، وكل ما توقف عليه الواجب فهو واجب، يُنتج: النظر واجب . الشنواني نقلا عن العدوي .
And the well-known from al-Ash'ari, the imam of Ahl al-Sunnah upon whose chosen opinion this poem was built, is that knowledge is the first obligation upon the mukallaf, because all obligations are not realized except through it. So be certain in your belief in it, and choose it without turning to others due to its preponderance. However, it is not attained except through reasoning; so reasoning is obligatory due to its obligation, because it depends upon it, while being within the capability of the mukallaf. And everything that is like that... (this is) the value of this statement. And the second matter: that he does not consider the disagreement of those who differ in these two matters, because not every disagreement is to be considered; only the disagreement that has a basis in reasoning is considered. Muhyi.

His statement: (because it depends upon it) meaning the dependence of knowledge upon reasoning. And his statement: (while being) meaning that reasoning is within the capability of the mukallaf. And you are aware that his statement (its dependence upon it... etc.) contains a syllogism of the first figure, its content: reasoning is depended upon by the obligatory knowledge, and everything upon which an obligatory depends is itself obligatory, therefore reasoning is obligatory. Al-Shanwani quoting from al-'Adawi.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage presents the well-known Ash'ari position that the first obligation is knowledge of Allah. The proof: all obligations depend on knowledge, so knowledge must be first. However, knowledge is only attained through nazar (reasoning), so nazar becomes obligatory as a means, provided it is within the mukallaf's ability. The commentator then reiterates that disagreements without sound basis are disregarded. The appended marginal note clarifies the logical proof: it is a first-figure syllogism—knowledge is obligatory; nazar is a necessary means (its dependence); therefore nazar is obligatory. This formalizes the argument.

معرفة
نظر
واجب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Nazar (reasoning) is not an obligation; it is optional or recommended.
Correction: The Ash'ari proof shows that because nazar is the only means to obligatory knowledge, it becomes obligatory itself as a necessary means.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari position is that knowledge of Allah is the first obligation, and reasoning becomes obligatory as its necessary means.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Reflecting on the Self: From Change to Creator
Commentary on the obligation of self-reflection and the argument from change to a Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how observing the changing states of your own self leads to certainty that you are created and require a Creator.

Core Idea
Your ever-changing self is evidence for an all-powerful Creator.

The commentator begins by urging reflection on one's own self as the closest reality to us. By carefully observing how our states constantly change, we realize that we are not self-sufficient but depend on an originator. This passage outlines the logical steps from change to a wise, necessary Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get its rhythm, then read the translation. Notice the direct address to the reader.
The passage uses 'you' to make it personal.
2
Identify the two reasons for self-reflection
The commentator lists two reasons: the self is the closest thing, and the hadith 'whoever knows himself knows his Lord.' Find these in the text.
3
Follow the argument from change
Observe the examples of changing states (health, sickness, etc.). Then trace the logical steps: change → originatedness of states → originatedness of self → need for an originator.
The conclusion is that the self requires a wise, necessary originator.
4
Reflect on the Quranic support
Note the Quranic verse cited (51:21) as a call to look within. How does it reinforce the argument?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النَّظرُ في النَّفْسِ والعَالَم بحيث يوصل إلى معرفة الله (إِلَى نَفْسِكَ) أي: في أحوالِ ذاتكَ (۱) ، لأنَّها أقرب الأشياء إليكَ ؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَفِي أَنفُسِكُمْ أَفَلَا تُبْصِرُونَ﴾ [الذاريات: ۲۱] ﴿وَلَقَدْ خَلَقْنَا الإِنسَانَ مِن سُلَلَةِ مِّن طِينِ﴾ [المؤمنون: ۱۲] فَتَستَدل بها = تری صحيح المصنف بذكر وجوب التفكر فى أحوال ذاته ؛ لأمور أولها أنها أقرب الأشياء إليه ، وثانيها: أنه قد ورد: «مَن عرف نفسه فقد عرف ربه أي من تفكر في بدائعها توصل إلى معرفة صانعها، وقيل معناه من عرف نفسه بالحدوث والفقر عرف ربه بالقدم والغنى، ، ألا أنك لو نظرت إلى أحوال ذاتك القريبة منك علمت أن أحوالها هذه متغيرة ، فأنت تارة معافى وتارة مريض، وأنت تارة واجد ما تريد، وتارة غير واجد لما تريد، وتارة قائم، وتارة قاعد، وتارة نائم، إلى غير ذلك من الأحوال التي تعرض لك ثم تزول، وإذا علمت أنها متغيرة أيقنت أنها حادثة ، وأيقنت مع ذلك أن ذاتك نفسها حادثة لأنها ملازمة لهذه الأمور الحادثة لا يمكن أن تنفك ذات عن بعض هذه الأمور ، وإذا علمت أن ذاتك وأحوالها حادثة أيقنت أن لا بد لها من محدث حكيم واجب الوجود عام العلم تام القدرة والإرادة ؛ فهذا طريق استدلال الإنسان بالنظر والتفكر في أحوال ذاته على وجوب وجود صانع حكيم، وكذلك التفكر في أحوال العالَميْنِ العلوي والسفلي فإنه سيجد كلا منهما مشمولا بجهات مخصوصة وأمكنة معينة، وسيجد بعضها متحركا وبعضها ساكنا، وبعضه نورانيا وبعضه ظلمانيا ، ، وسيجد أن هذه الأحوال تتغير إلى مقابلاتها، وهذا أمارة الحدوث، وهو أمارة الاحتياج إلى مُحدث، وقد نعى الله تعالى على من ترك الفكر في أمر نفسه بقوله سبحانه: ﴿وَفِي أَنفُسِكُمْ أَفَلَا تُبْصِرُونَ﴾ [الذاريات: ۲۱] يريد سبحانه ـ والله أعلم ـ وفي أنفسكم آيات دالة على حدوثكم وافتقاركم إلى محدث يوجد، متصف بجميع صفات الكمال ، أتتركون التفكر فيها فلا تبصرون. وهذا الاستفهام بمعنى النفي، أي: لا ينبغي لكم ترك النظر في أحوال ذواتكم، ونبّه سبحانه على وجوب التفكر في العالمين العلوي والسفلي بقوله: ﴿إِنَّ فِي خَلْقِ السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ وَاخْتِلَفِ الَّيْلِ وَالنَّهَارِ وَالْفُلْكِ الَّتِي تَجْرِي فِي الْبَحْرِ بِمَا يَنفَعُ النَّاسَ وَمَا أَنزَلَ اللَّهُ مِنَ السَّمَاءِ مِن مَّاءٍ فَأَحْيَا بِهِ الْأَرْضَ بَعْدَ مَوْتِهَا وَبَثَّ فِيهَا مِن كُلِّ دَابَّةٍ وَتَصْرِيفِ الرِّيحِ وَالسَّحَابِ الْمُسَخَّرِ بَيْنَ السَّمَاءِ وَالْأَرْضِ لَآيَاتٍ لِقَوْمٍ يَعْقِلُونَ﴾ [البقرة: ١٦٤] . محي.
Speculation about the soul and the world such that it leads to knowledge of God. (To yourself) i.e., about the states of your own self (dhatika), because they are the closest things to you; due to His statement the Exalted: {And in yourselves, do you not see?} [Quran 51:21] {And indeed We created man from an extract of clay} [Quran 23:12]. So you infer by them. You see correctly: The author mentioned the obligation of reflection upon the states of one's self for several reasons: first, because they are the closest things to him; second, because it has been reported: 'Whoever knows himself knows his Lord' i.e., whoever reflects upon its wonders arrives at knowledge of its Maker, and it is said its meaning is whoever knows himself by originatedness (al-huduth) and poverty knows his Lord by pre-eternity (al-qidam) and richness. Indeed, if you look at the states of your own self close to you, you know that these states of yours are changing; you are sometimes healthy and sometimes sick, sometimes obtaining what you want and sometimes not obtaining, sometimes standing, sometimes sitting, sometimes sleeping, and so on of the states that occur to you and then vanish. And when you know that they are changing you become certain that they are originated (haditha), and you become certain along with that that your very self is originated because it is inseparable from these originated matters—a self cannot be free of some of these matters. And when you know that your self and its states are originated, you become certain that it necessarily has an originator (muhdith) who is wise, necessary of existence, universal in knowledge, perfect in power and will. So this is the way of a person's inference by speculation and reflection upon the states of his self to the necessary existence of a wise Maker. Likewise, reflection upon the states of the two worlds, the higher and the lower, for he will find each of them encompassed by specific directions and fixed places, and he will find some of them moving and some stationary, some luminous and some dark, and he will find that these states change into their opposites. And this is a sign of originatedness, and it is a sign of need for an originator. And God the Exalted has censured those who abandon reflection upon their own selves by His saying: {And in yourselves, do you not see?} —He intends, and God knows best, 'And in yourselves are signs indicating your originatedness and your need for an originator who exists, characterized by all attributes of perfection; do you leave reflection upon them and thus do not see?' And this question is in the sense of negation, i.e., you should not leave speculation about the states of your selves. And He the Exalted alerted to the obligation of reflection upon the two worlds, the higher and the lower, by His saying: {Indeed in the creation of the heavens and the earth and the alternation of the night and the day and the ships which sail in the sea with what benefits people, and what God has sent down from the sky of water, and thereby revived the earth after its death, and dispersed in it every kind of creature, and the directing of the winds and the clouds subservient between the heaven and the earth are signs for people who reason} [Quran 2:164].
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the author's statement that speculation about the soul and the world leads to knowledge of God. It begins by explaining the phrase 'to yourself' as meaning 'about the states of your own self', because the self is the closest thing to a person, citing Quran 51:21 and 23:12. The commentator then notes that the author mentioned the obligation of reflection on one's states for two reasons: first, because they are nearest; second, because the hadith 'whoever knows himself knows his Lord' means that reflecting on the wonders of one's self leads to knowledge of its Maker, or that knowing one's own originatedness and poverty leads to knowing God's pre-eternity and richness. The commentator then provides a step-by-step argument: observe the changing states of the self (health/sickness, possession/lack, posture, sleep). Since these states are changing, they are originated (haditha). Since the self is inseparable from these originated states, the self itself is originated. Therefore, the self necessarily has an originator who is wise, necessary, all-knowing, and all-powerful. This is the inferential path from self-reflection to God's existence. The same reasoning applies to the universe: observing the heavens and earth, their varying conditions (movement, stillness, light, dark), and their changes, one concludes they are originated and need an originator. Finally, the commentator explains Quranic verses: 51:21 rebukes those who neglect reflection; 2:164 commands reflection on cosmic phenomena. The passage thus presents a complete kalam argument from change and contingency to the existence of God.

Key Terms

النفس
الحدوث
المحدث
القديم
واجب الوجود
التفكر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that self-knowledge is merely introspective or psychological, not a logical proof for God.
Correction: The commentator insists that observing one's changing states is an inferential argument that leads to certainty of a Creator, not just introspection.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the first step in inferring a Creator from oneself?
A) Observing the vastness of the universe.
B) Noticing that one's own states change.
C) Reading about God in scriptures.
D) Consulting scholars.

Key Takeaway
The changing states of your own self prove that you are created and require a wise, necessary Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a sudden change in your health, mood, or circumstances, do you immediately see it as a sign of your own createdness and need for Allah? What prevents you from making this reflection a constant habit, and what one change will you make today to begin?
?
Consider a recent moment of weakness or illness: did you react with frustration or with recognition of your limits and the Creator's power? How can you train yourself to see each change as a fresh proof of your dependence on Him?
?
If your very existence at this moment depends entirely on an Originator who is all-wise and all-powerful, what does that demand of your relationship with Him? What specific action will you take within the next hour to acknowledge that dependence?

Reflecting on the Universe: Signs of a Creator
Commentary on the obligation of cosmic reflection and the argument from change to a Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how observing the constant changes in the universe leads to the conclusion that it has a Creator.

Core Idea
The universe's ceaseless change proves its need for an originator.

After establishing the method from self-reflection, the commentator extends the same reasoning to the entire cosmos. He points to the heavens, earth, and all phenomena as displaying variation and change, which are signs of originatedness and thus of a Wise Originator. Quranic verses reinforce this obligation to reflect.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage for the cosmic argument, then read the translation. Notice the shift from self to the universe.
The passage starts with 'Likewise' connecting it to the previous argument.
2
Identify the observations of the universe
Look for the examples: directions, places, motion, stillness, light, dark, and their changes. List them.
3
Trace the logical conclusion
From these observations, the commentator concludes that the universe is originated (haditha) and thus needs an originator. Follow this logic.
The change to opposites is key.
4
Understand the Quranic verses
The passage cites two verses: 51:21 as censure for neglecting self-reflection, and 2:164 as a command to reflect on cosmic signs. See how they support the argument.
The questioning in 51:21 is rhetorical negation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness the daily fade of light into darkness and the return of dawn, do you truly feel your own dependency on the One who orchestrates that change, or does it pass as a routine spectacle? ?

Do you live as if you are a self-made project, or as someone who is constantly upheld by an Originator? What would change if you fully owned your originatedness in every decision today?
?

If you were to take five minutes after this lesson to honestly reflect on one change in your own body or life as a sign pointing to its Maker, what would you contemplate and what would you say to Him?

The Upper World's Contingency
Al-Sharqawi describes the upper world's properties to demonstrate its contingency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the observable properties of celestial bodies demonstrate their contingency and need for a transcendent Creator.

Core Idea
The heavens are not eternal—they bear signs of limitation and change.

After considering the soul, the next step in proving God is to examine the world around us. The author focuses on the 'upper world'—the heavens, stars, and celestial bodies. By observing their specific directions, varying states, and constant change, we can see they are not self-existent but require a Maker who is utterly unlike them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the flow: from defining the world to listing properties of the upper world.
The passage ends mid-sentence introducing the lower world; focus on the upper world for now.
2
Identify the properties of the upper world
Find the specific characteristics mentioned: encompassed by directions, particular places, motion and stillness, light and darkness.
These are empirical observations available to anyone.
3
Connect properties to contingency
Reflect on why these properties imply origination (ḥudūth). Anything limited, changing, or dependent cannot be eternal or self-sufficient.
This is the core of the proof.
4
Understand the conclusion
The passage concludes that the upper world needs a Maker who is transcendent (munazzah) and unlike His creation in essence and attributes.
This preserves divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النَّظرُ في النَّفْسِ والعَالَم بحيثُ يوصل إلى معرفة الله (ثُمَّ انْتَقِلِ) بعد نظركَ في نفسكَ (لِلْعَالَمِ) أي: للنظر في أحوال العالم (العُلْوِيَّ) وهو ما سوى الله تعالى وصفاته من الموجودات، سُمِّي به : لأنَّه علمٌ على وجود الصانع تعالى ؛ فيُعلم به ، ويُستَدَلَّ به عليه؛ لأنَّ في كلِّ علامةً کل تدلّ على قدرة الصانع وإرادته وعلمه وحياته وحكمته، والمراد بالعلوي: ما ارتفع من الفلكيَّات من سماواتٍ وكواكب وغيرها؛ لأنَّك تجده مشمولا لجهاتٍ مخصوصةٍ وأمكنة مُعيَّنة، وبعضه ،متحرّكَاً ، وبعضه ساكنا، وبعضه نورانيا، وبعضه ظُلمانيا، وذلك دليل الحدوث والافتقار إلى صانع مُنزّه عن مماثلة لمصنوعه ذاتا وصفات. (ثُمَّ انتقل بالنظر في أحوال العالم (السفلي) وهو كلُّ ما نَزل عن
The consideration of the soul and the world such that it leads to knowledge of God. (Then move on) after your consideration of your soul (to the world), i.e., to consider the states of the world (the upper world), which is everything other than God Almighty and His attributes among existing things. It is named thus because it is a sign for the existence of the Maker Almighty; so He is known by it, and it is used as evidence for Him, because in everything there is a sign indicating His power, will, knowledge, life, and wisdom. By the upper world is meant what is high among the celestial bodies like the heavens, stars, and others, because you find it encompassed by specific directions and particular places, some of it moving, some stationary, some luminous, some dark; and that is evidence of origination (al-ḥudūth) and need for a Maker transcendent above resemblance to His creation in essence and attributes. (Then move on) by considering the states of the (lower world), which is everything that descends from...
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage continues the method of proving God by turning from the soul to the world, specifically the 'upper world' (al-‘ālam al-‘ulwī). First, it defines the world as everything other than God and His attributes. The upper world is named for being a sign of the Maker. The passage then describes the observable properties of celestial bodies: they are confined to specific directions and places, some move, some are stationary, some luminous, some dark. These properties demonstrate limitation, change, and dependence, which are signs of origination (ḥudūth). Therefore, the upper world requires a Creator who is absolutely transcendent (munazzah) and unlike His creation. The passage then introduces the lower world (al-‘ālam al-sufalī) as another domain for consideration, but the present focus is on the upper world's contingency.

Key Terms
العالم
العلوي
الصانع
الحدوث
العلوي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The heavens are eternal and self-existent.
Correction: The passage shows that celestial bodies have specific directions, places, motion, stillness, light, and darkness, all of which indicate change and dependence, proving they are contingent and created.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider the upper world to be contingent?
A) It is eternal.
B) It is self-existent.
C) It has specific directions, places, and varying states.
D) It is not created.

Key Takeaway
The upper world's change and limitation prove it is created and needs a transcendent Maker.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the stars or the sky, do you ever feel a sense of awe mixed with neglect? What specific step can you take this week to regularly reflect on the upper world as a sign of Allah, rather than just a backdrop to your life?
?
The passage says the Maker is transcendent, unlike His creation. In what subtle ways have you been imagining Allah to be similar to creation (e.g., needing rest, having a body, being limited to a place)? Write down one mistaken belief you will actively work to correct starting today.
?
You just learned that the heavens—vast and orderly—are contingent and dependent on Allah. How does that truth challenge your own sense of self-sufficiency? What is one area of your life where you have been acting as if you are independent, and how will you consciously remind yourself of your need for Allah?

The Poem: Discovery of Wisdom and the Proof from Contingency
Poem couplet with commentary on design and contingency proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how reflecting on the perfect design of creation reveals both the wisdom of the Creator and the contingency of the creature, and how this establishes the proof of God's existence.

Core Idea
Contemplating creation's perfect design reveals both the Creator's wisdom and the creature's contingency.

This lesson unpacks a key couplet from the Jawharat al-Tawhid. The poet invites us to reflect on the states of the self and the universe. In doing so, we discover a 'wonderful craftsmanship' that points directly to the Maker's attributes. At the same time, this same observation leads to the realization that everything besides Allah is contingent, needing a Necessary Being. This dual insight is the core of the proof from contingency.

Guided Path
1
Read the poem line and translation
Start by reading the Arabic couplet and its English translation carefully. Notice the conditional structure: 'if you reflect... you will find...'
2
Understand 'wonderful craftsmanship'
The commentary explains that 'wonderful wisdom' (بديع الحكم) means perfection (إتقان), which indicates the Maker's knowledge, power, and will. Reflect on how design implies a designer.
3
Connect to the proof of contingency
The second hemistich states 'but through it stands the proof of contingency.' Consider why the same observation that shows design also shows that the created thing is not self-sufficient—it depends on a necessary being.
The commentary in this lesson only expands the first hemistich, but the overall argument is clear.
4
Synthesize the argument
Bring the two insights together: The intricate design of creation points to a wise Creator, and the contingency of creation points to a Necessary Being. Together they form a complete proof for God's existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَإِنَّكَ إِنْ تَنْظُرْ فِي أَحْوَالِ مَا ذُكِرَ تَجِدْ بِهِ صُنْعًا بَدِيعَ الْحِكَمِ لَكِنْ بِهِ قَامَ دَلِيلُ الْعَدَمِ فَإِنَّكَ إِنْ تَنْظُرُ فِي أَحْوَالِ مَا ذُكِرَ تَجِدْ بِهِ أَيْ تَعْلَمُ وَتَتَحَقَّقُ فِيمَا ذُكِرَ صُنْعًا بَدِيعَ الْحِكَمِ أَيِ الْإِتْقَانِ الدَّالَّ عَلَى عِلْمِ صَانِعِهِ وَقُدْرَتِهِ وَإِرَادَتِهِ
For if you reflect upon the states of what has been mentioned, you will find through it a craftsmanship of wonderful wisdom, but through it stands the proof of contingency. For if you reflect upon the states of what has been mentioned, you will find through it—i.e., you will know and realize in what has been mentioned—a craftsmanship of wonderful wisdom, i.e., of perfection, indicating the knowledge of its Maker, His power, and His will.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage quotes a line from the poem (matn) of Jawharat al-Tawhid and begins its explanation. The poem states that if one considers the conditions of the things mentioned earlier (the self and the two worlds), one will discover a 'craftsmanship of wonderful wisdom' (ṣunʿan badīʿ al-ḥikam). The phrase 'wonderful wisdom' means perfect design (itqān), and this design points to the knowledge, power, and will of the Maker. The commentary then unpacks the first hemistich: 'you will find through it' means you will come to know and verify the existence of such divine attributes. The second hemistich, 'but through it stands the proof of contingency' (lakin bihī qāma dalīl al-ʿadam), indicates that the very same observation of perfect design leads to the realization that the created thing is contingent—it is not self-sufficient but depends on a necessary being. The commentary provided in this lesson only covers the first hemistich; however the overall argument is clear: contemplating the intricate design of creation establishes both the perfect wisdom of the Creator and the creature's contingency, which collectively prove the existence of God as the necessary Maker.

Key Terms
صُنْع (ṣunʿ)
بديع الحكم (badīʿ al-ḥikam)
الإتقان (al-itqān)
دليل العدم (dalīl al-ʿadam)

A) The beauty of nature
B) A craftsmanship of wonderful wisdom
C) The soul's immortality
D) The laws of physics

Key Takeaway
Reflecting on the perfect design of creation simultaneously reveals the wisdom of the Creator and the creature's contingency, forming a powerful proof for God's existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own soul—its desires, thoughts, and limitations—do you truly see it as a sign of your contingency, or do you live as if your existence is self-evident? What would change in your daily life if you constantly reminded yourself that you could just as easily not exist?
?
The poem invites you to find 'wonderful wisdom' in creation. When was the last time you stopped to actually wonder at the precision of a single flower or the order of your own heartbeat? Does your lack of wonder reveal a hardness of heart that takes God’s gifts for granted?
?
If both design and contingency are equally visible in everything around you, why do you think you so often overlook the contingency? Are you afraid to admit that you are utterly dependent on a Being who is not you? How would your prayers and reliance on God change if you fully embraced this?

Constructing the Syllogism for the Temporality of the World
The commentator explains the arrangement of premises for the syllogism proving the world's temporality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how to construct a syllogism to prove that the world is originated and in need of an effecter.

Core Idea
The world's originated accidents prove its temporality, which leads to the need for a Creator.

In this lesson, we follow the commentator's explanation of a classic kalam argument. We start with the observation that the world is inseparable from changing accidents, then we apply a logical rule to conclude its temporality. This syllogism is a cornerstone of Islamic theology for proving the existence of God.

Guided Path
1
Understand the minor premise
Identify that the first part of the syllogism is the minor premise: the world is liable to non-being because it has originated accidents like motion and rest. These accidents are signs of contingency.
The text says 'the world, from its Throne to its carpet, is liable to non-being.'
2
Understand the major premise
The major premise is that everything that admits non-being cannot be eternal. This is a logical rule: if something can cease to exist, it cannot have existed eternally.
3
See the conclusion
From the two premises, the conclusion follows: the world is originated (ḥādith). This is the core result.
The text explicitly states: 'So that yields: that the world is originated.'
4
Explore the alternative syllogism
The commentator also presents an alternative formulation: the world is in need of an effecter because it is originated, and every originated thing has an effecter. This directly leads to the existence of a Creator.
This alternative is a slightly different approach that leads to the same goal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كيفية ترتيب المقدمات لإثبات حدوث العالم وحياته ، واختياره ؛ لأنَّ الإتقان لا يصدرُ إِلَّا عَمَّنْ اتّصفَ بما ذُكر . وما يُشعر به قوله: «بديع الحكم من قدمه، حيث كان كذلك .. يدفعه الاستدراك بقوله: (لَكِنْ) العالَمُ وإن كانَ على غاية من الإتقان هو حادث ؛ لأَنَّه (به) لا بغيره (۱) (قَامَ دَلِيلُ ) أي : أَمَارَةُ (العَدَم) وهي الأعراض الحادثة المُلازمة له، كالحركة والسُّكون التي لا تقوم بغير الحادثِ ؛ فإذا أردتَ أنْ تأتي بقياس مستنبط من نظركَ في العالم لتتوصل به إلى تحقق حدوثه ، قلت: العالم مِن عرشه لفَرشِه جائز عليه العدم» وهذه المقدّمة الصُّغرى المطويَّةُ لفهمها من الاستدراك، وبيان هذه المقدمة : أنا اختبرنا الموجود(۲) من العالم فوجدناه غير خارج عن الأعيان والأعراض، وهي حادثة لقبولها للعدم، ولو كانت قديمة .. ما طرأ العدم عليها ، والمقدمة الكبرى هي قوله: كيفية ترتيب المقدمات لإثبات حدوث العالم وافتقاره إلى مؤثر] ١٧ - وكُلُّ ما جَازَ عَلَيْهِ العَدَمُ عَلَيْهِ قَطْعًا يَسْتَحِيْلُ القِدَمُ ۱۷ (وَكُلُّ مَا جَازَ عَلَيْهِ العَدَمُ يعني الفَنَاءُ عَلَيْهِ قَطْعًا يَسْتَحِيلُ) أي: يمتنع (القدمُ) فيُنتج ذلك: أنَّ العالَمَ حادِثٌ . وإن شئتَ قلت: «العَالَم مفتقر إلى مؤثر(۳) ، (۳) ، لأنَّه مُحدَثٌ ، وكلُّ مُحْدَثٍ فله (مُؤثّر فيُنتج القياسُ : أنَّ العالم له مؤثر .
Explanation of the arrangement of premises to prove the temporality of the world and its life and choice; because perfect workmanship only comes from one who is described with what was mentioned. And what is implied by his saying: 'The wonderful wisdom from His eternity, as it was so...' is rebutted by the exception with his saying: 'But the world, even if it is at the utmost perfection, is originated; because the evidence of non-being (al-ʿadam) is established for it (bi) not by other than it (1) — that is, the sign of non-being, which are the originated accidents (al-aʿrāḍ al-ḥāditha) that are inseparable from it, like motion and rest, which do not subsist in other than the originated. So if you want to bring a syllogism derived from your reflection on the world to reach through it to verifying its temporality, you say: The world, from its Throne to its carpet, is liable to non-being.’ And this is the minor premise (al-muqaddima al-ṣughrā) implied to be understood from the exception. The clarification of this premise is: We have tested the existent from the world and found it not free from substances and accidents, and they are originated because they accept non-being, and if they were eternal, non-being would not have occurred to them. And the major premise (al-muqaddima al-kubrā) is his saying: [How to arrange premises to prove the temporality of the world and its need for an effecter] 17 - ‘And everything that admits non-being is eternally necessarily inappropriate for pre-eternity (al-qidam).’ 17 Explanation: (And everything that admits non-being, meaning annihilation (al-fanā’), is necessarily impossible (i.e., unreal) for pre-eternity. So that yields: that the world is originated. And if you wish, you say: ‘The world is in need of an effecter (muʾaththir), because it is originated, and every originated thing has an effecter.’ So the syllogism yields: that the world has an effecter.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage is a proof (istidlāl) for the temporality of the world. The commentator explains the steps to construct a syllogism. First, he clarifies the minor premise: the world is inseparable from originated accidents, so it is contingent. Then the major premise: whatever is contingent cannot be eternal. The conclusion is that the world is originated. Alternatively, one can argue that the world needs an effecter because it is originated, and every originated thing has an effecter. This is a classic kalam argument for the existence of God.

Key Terms

حادث
القدم
العدم
الأعراض
مؤثر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the proof is circular because it assumes that the world's accidents are originated.
Correction: The passage clarifies that this is based on observation: we have tested the existent world and found that accidents accept non-being (they cease), so they are originated by empirical evidence, not assumption.

Checkpoint
What is the minor premise in the syllogism to prove the world is originated?
A) The world is eternal.
B) The world has originated accidents.
C) The world is independent.
D) The world is perfect.

Key Takeaway
The world's temporality is proven by the originated accidents inseparable from it, leading to the conclusion that it has an effecter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof shows that everything in the world, including your own body, is constantly accompanied by change and is liable to non-being. When you look at your life, do you truly feel this fragility, or do you live as if you will never cease? What one attachment are you clinging to that you need to release in light of this truth?
?
The world cannot be eternal because it admits non-being. What does that mean for your future hopes and fears? Are you building your life on something that will last or on something that will inevitably pass? Write down one specific way you will shift your priorities today because of this.
?
The syllogism concludes that the world has an effecter. Do you live as if you have an effecter who is responsible for your existence and sustenance, or do you act as if you are self-sufficient? What is one decision you will make today that acknowledges your total dependence on God?

Proof of the Necessity of God's Existence from Contingency
Ithaaf al-Mureed presents a concise proof for the necessity of God's existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the necessity of God's existence is proven through the contingency of the world and why a possible being cannot be the ultimate cause.

Core Idea
If the world depends on something, that something must be necessary, not possible.

This passage from Ithaaf al-Mureed presents a concise yet powerful proof for the necessity of God's existence. It begins with the premise that the entire world is contingent – it depends on something else. The author then argues that the being on which the world depends cannot itself be contingent, otherwise an infinite regress would result. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic source text and its literal translation. Notice the key terms: 'iftiqar' (dependence), 'wajib al-wujud' (necessary existence), 'dawr' (circularity), and 'tasalsul' (infinite regress).
2
Identify the two premises
The proof rests on two premises: (1) the world and every part of it depends on God, and (2) the being upon whom the world depends must have necessary existence. Locate these in the text.
The second premise is justified by the avoidance of circularity or infinite regress.
3
Understand the reasoning against possibility
Consider why the being cannot be merely possible (ja'iz). If it were possible, it would need a cause, leading to either circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul), both of which are rationally impossible.
This reasoning is the core of the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم الإلهيات، ولا أبدا؛ لوجوب افتقار العالم وكلّ جزء من أجزائه إليه تعالى وكُلٌّ مَن وجب افتقارُ العالم إليه لا يكونُ وجوده إلَّا واجبا، لا جائزاً؛ وإلَّا لزمَ الدَّورُ أو التّسلسلُ.
Section of Ilahiyyat. And never; because of the necessity of the world's dependence and every part of its parts on Him, exalted is He, and everyone upon whom the world's dependence is necessary, his existence can only be necessary, not possible; otherwise circularity or infinite regress would follow.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage presents a concise proof for the necessity of God's existence. It begins with the section heading 'Ilahiyyat' (divine matters). Then it states that the world and every part of it is dependent on God. This is the premise of contingency (iftiqar). It then argues that the being upon whom the world depends must exist necessarily, not possibly. The reason given is that if that being were possible, then it would need another to bring it into existence, leading to either circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul), both of which are invalid. Thus, the conclusion is that God's existence is necessary (wajib al-wujud).

Key Terms
قسم الإلهيات
افتقار
وجوب الوجود
إمكان
دور
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof requires accepting the existence of a contingent world, which the skeptic denies.
Correction: The proof does not assume the world's existence; it argues from the observed contingency of the world to the necessity of a non-contingent cause. Even a skeptic who questions the world's existence would have to account for why anything exists at all.

Checkpoint
What does the proof in this passage argue about the existence of the being upon whom the world depends?
A) It must be possible.
B) It must be necessary.
C) It can be either necessary or possible.
D) It is impossible.

Key Takeaway
The necessity of God's existence is proven by the impossibility of an infinite regress of causes, as the world's contingency demands a necessary being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live as though you are completely dependent on God in every breath, or do you sometimes act as if you are self-sufficient? What specific habit would change if you fully internalized that you are contingent and He is the only Necessary Being?
?
This argument shows that without a Necessary Being, you would have no ultimate grounding—your existence would be an endless chain of causes. How does that realization make you feel about your purpose? Will you let it drive you to seek the One who is the foundation of all reality?
?
If God's existence is necessary, then ignoring Him is not an option—it is a denial of reality itself. In what area of your life have you been treating God as optional rather than necessary? What is one concrete step you will take this week to acknowledge His necessity in that area?
