The Two Views on Existence: Identity vs. Addition
Presents two scholarly opinions on existence: identity or addition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main scholarly opinions on whether existence is identical to the essence or additional to it, and the implications for describing Allah.

Core Idea
Is existence just the essence itself, or something extra? Two views change how we talk about Allah.

This lesson tackles a foundational question in Islamic theology: When we say 'Allah exists,' does existence add anything to His essence? The passage presents the debate between the Ash'ari school, which holds identity, and the Razi school, which posits existence as an additional attribute. Understanding this helps clarify how theologians use the term 'attribute' for divine existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage's framing
Notice the author begins by stating three matters needed to discuss existence. Only the first is explored here.
This sets the context for the detailed debate.
2
Identify the two positions
Find where al-Ash'ari's view is stated and where al-Razi's view begins. Note how each defines the relationship between existence and essence.
The key terms are 'ayn al-dhat' (identical) and 'za'id 'ala al-dhat' (additional).
3
Understand the classification of attributes
The Ash'ari view says existence is not a real attribute, only called so by analogy. The Razi view makes it a 'state' (hal) between existent and non-existent.
4
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: How does each view affect how we describe Allah? Does calling existence an attribute change its meaning?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القول في الوجود يحتاج إلى بيان ثلاثة أمور: الأول: معنى الوجود، والثاني: معنى وجوبه له سبحانه، والثالث: الدليل على وجوب وجود الله تعالى. فأما الكلام على الأول فيستدعي أن نُقدّم لك بحثًا يترتب عليه بيان معنى الوجود، وهذا البحث يتلخص في أنَّ الوجود مطلقا ـ نعني سواء أكان صفةٌ للقديم أم كان صفة للحادث - هل هو عين الذات الموجودة أو هو غير الذات؟ وقد اختلف العلماء في هذه المسألة، فذهب أبو الحسن الأشعري: إلى أن الوجود هو نفس الذات، وعلى هذا لا يقال إن الوجود صفة، لأن الصفة اسم للأمر الزائد على الموصوف، وعلى هذا يكون عد المتكلمين والمؤلفين المصنف والشارح الوجود من الصفات الواجبة له سبحانه مشتملا على شيء من التّسامح، وسببه: أنهم رأوا أنه يصح وصف ذات الله تعالى لفظا بما يُشتق من الوجود، فيقال: «ذات الله موجودة» فلما رأوا أنَّ الوجود يكون وصفًا في اللفظ كالصفة الحقيقية في نحو قولنا: الله تعالى عالم وقادر أطلقوا لفظ الصفة على الوجود تشبيها له بها. وقال قوم منهم الرَّازِيُّ: الوجود أمر زائد على الذات، على معنى أنه صفة ثابتة في الخارج لكنها لم تصل إلى درجة الموجودات التي تشاهد وتحسُّ، فهي واسطة بين الموجود الخارجي والمعدوم، وهذا الفريق أثبت واسطة بين الموجود والمعدوم، وسمّي هذه الواسطة عنده ثلاثة: معدوم، وموجود، وحالٌ. أما الفريق الأول فليس عنده إلا حالا، فالأقسام قسمان: الموجود والمعدوم.
The discussion on existence requires clarification of three matters: First: the meaning of existence, Second: the meaning of its necessity for Him, exalted, and Third: the proof for the necessity of the existence of Allah, exalted. As for speaking on the first, it requires that we present to you an investigation upon which the clarification of the meaning of existence depends, and this investigation is summarized in that existence absolutely — meaning whether it be an attribute of the eternal or an attribute of the originated — is it identical to the existing essence or is it other than the essence? And the scholars have differed in this issue: Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari held that existence is identical to the essence, and on this basis it is not said that existence is an attribute, because an attribute is a name for something additional to the described. On this basis, the enumeration by the theologians and authors — the author and the commentator — of existence among the necessary attributes of Him, exalted, involves some indulgence. The reason is that they saw that it is valid to describe the essence of Allah, exalted, verbally with what is derived from existence, so it is said: 'The essence of Allah is existent.' So when they saw that existence is a description in speech like a real attribute in something like our saying: Allah, exalted, is knowing and powerful, they applied the term 'attribute' to existence by analogy with it. And some people, among them al-Razi, said: Existence is something additional to the essence, meaning that it is an attribute established in the external world but it has not reached the level of existents that are observed and sensed; it is an intermediary between the external existent and the non-existent. This group affirmed an intermediary between the existent and the non-existent, and they named this intermediary three: non-existent, existent, and state (hal). As for the first group, they have only two: for them, the divisions are two: the existent and the non-existent.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage introduces the topic of existence (al-wujud) by stating it requires clarification of three matters: meaning, necessity, and proof. Focusing on the first, the author presents the central question: Is existence identical to the essence or other than it? The Ash'ari view holds identity: existence is the essence itself, so it is not a real attribute but is sometimes called an attribute by verbal analogy. Al-Razi's view holds that existence is an additional attribute, a 'state' (hal) that is an intermediary between existent and non-existent. This introduces a threefold division (existent, non-existent, state) versus the twofold division (existent and non-existent) of the first group.

Key Terms
الوجود (al-wujud)
عين الذات (ayn al-dhat)
زائد على الذات (za'id 'ala al-dhat)
الحال (al-hal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Existence is always a real attribute like knowledge or power.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari holds existence is identical to essence, so it is not a real attribute additional to the essence. The term 'attribute' is used only by verbal analogy.

Checkpoint
What is the position of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari regarding existence?
A) Existence is identical to the essence
B) Existence is an additional attribute
C) Existence is a state between existent and non-existent
D) Existence is a created accident

Key Takeaway
The debate on whether existence is identical to essence or additional to it divides theologians, with Ash'ari affirming identity and Razi affirming addition, affecting how we classify divine attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah exists,' do you treat that existence as a fact separate from His essence, or as the very reality of who He is? How might your answer change the way you approach Him in prayer?
?
The scholars differed on whether existence is an attribute. Do you ever assume your own understanding of God is the only valid one? How do you react when you encounter deep theological disagreements among trustworthy scholars?
?
If existence is identical to Allah's essence, then His existence is not just a property but His very being. Does that make you feel closer to Him, or more in awe? What step can you take this week to remind yourself that Allah's existence is not a mere fact but the foundation of all reality?

The First Proof: Existence as Addition Leads to Regress or Contradiction
Presents the first proof with two branches.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the reductio argument used to prove that existence must be identical to essence.

Core Idea
The same infinite regress that keeps existence forever escaping itself.

The debate over whether existence is identical to or additional to the essence begins with a clever logical trap. If existence were added to the essence, what would be the status of that added existence? The proof uses this question to force an impossible choice: either an infinite regress or a direct contradiction. Let's examine how this argument works.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Focus on the structure: the proof presents two exclusive possibilities for the supposed additional existence. Notice how each leads to an absurdity.
2
Follow the two branches
First branch: if the additional existence is itself existent, we ask about its existence, leading to regress. Second branch: if it is non-existent, we describe existence as non-existent—a contradiction.
The conclusion is that existence cannot be additional.
3
Reflect on the method
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum. The value of this argument is in its logical form, which will be refuted in the next lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد استدل القائلون بأن الوجود عين الموجود بحجج كثيرة نجتزئ لك منها بدليلين:
الأوَّل: قالوا لو كان الوجود أمراً زائداً على الموجود لكان حال هذا الوجود الزائد لا يخلو عن أحد أمرين: إما أن يكون موجوداً، وإما أن يكون معدوماً، فإن كان موجوداً انتقل الكلام إلى وجوده، وهكذا؛ فيلزم عليه الدور أو التسلسل وكلاهما باطل، فما أدى إليه وهو كون الوجود أمراً موجوداً زائداً على الذات باطل، وإن كان الوجود معدوماً لزم اتصاف الشيء الذي هو الوجود بنقيضه فيقال: «الوجود معدوم» وذلك محال لأنه جمع بين النقيضين. ويمكن أن يجاب عن هذا الدليل بأحد جوابين:
أولهما: أنا لا نسلم الحصر في قولكم: إن كان هذا الوجود زائدا على الذات فلا يخلو حاله إما أن يكون موجوداً وإما أن يكون معدوما بل ندعي أنه زائد على الذات ومع ذلك فليس موجوداً ولا معدوماً، لأن القسمة ليست ثنائية عندهم على ما علمت، بل هناك قسم ثالث وهو: الحال.
والجواب الثاني: سلمنا أنه على ما ذكرتم، ولكنا نختار الشَّقَّ الثَّاني، وهو أنه معدوم، ولا نسلم أنه على فرض كونه معدوماً يستلزم وصف الشيء بنقيضه وأنه محال، لأن المحال إنَّما وصف الشَّيء بعين نقيضه كقولنا: الوجود عدم وقولنا: الموجود معدوم»، وأما وصف الشيء بالمنسوب إلى نقيضه، كقولنا: الوجود عدمي»، أو بالمشتق من نقيضه كقولنا: الوجود معدوم فلا استحالة فيه، وما هنا من هذا القبيل؛ لأنَّنا نقول: «الوجود معدوم» أو «الوجود عدمي» على معنى أنه لا تحقق له في الخارج، ولا يلزمُ مِن ذلك أنْ تكونَ الذَّات المتصفة بالوجود معدومةً، لأنَّ العدم قائمٌ بوصفها - وهو الوجود ـ لا بنفس الذات، ولهذا نظائر في كلامنا، ألا تری أنَّ الإنسان يتصف بالقيام والقعود، والبياض والسواد، ونحو ذلك. ثمَّ إن الإنسان جسمٌ وجوهرٌ، وكل ما يتصف الإنسان به عرض ليس بجسم ولا جوهر فإذا قلت: «زيد قائم» أو «زيد أبيض» فقد وصفت الجسم الذي هو الإنسان بما ليس بجسم، ففيه وصف الشَّيء بنقيضه، لكن لما كان المعنى على أنَّ الإنسان الذي هو جسم صاحب شيئا ليس بجسم كان معنى صحيحاً، وما هنا من هذا القبيل، فإنك لو قلت: «الذات موجودة مع قولنا بأن الوجود معدوم يصير المعنى الذاتُ الموجودة خارجا متصفةٌ بشيء ليس موجودا في الخارج، ولا شيء في هذا.
And those who say that existence is identical to the existent have argued with many proofs, of which we will present two for you: The first: They said: If existence were something additional to the existent, then the state of this additional existence would not be free from one of two matters: either it is existent, or it is non-existent. If it is existent, then the discussion moves to its existence, and so on; thus circularity or infinite regress follows, both of which are false. So what leads to that—namely, existence being an existent thing additional to the essence—is false. And if existence is non-existent, then it would entail describing the thing which is existence by its opposite, so it would be said: 'Existence is non-existent,' and that is impossible because it combines opposites. And this proof can be answered with one of two answers: The first of them: We do not accept the exclusive division in your statement: If this existence is additional to the essence, then its state is not free from either being existent or being non-existent. Rather, we claim that it is additional to the essence and yet it is neither existent nor non-existent, because the division is not binary according to them as you have learned; rather, there is a third division, which is: the state (hal). The second answer: We grant what you mentioned, but we choose the second branch, namely that it is non-existent, and we do not accept that on the assumption of its being non-existent it entails describing the thing by its opposite and that it is impossible. For impossibility is only describing the thing by its exact opposite, like our saying: 'Existence is non-existence' and our saying: 'The existent is non-existent.' As for describing the thing by what is related to its opposite, like our saying: 'Existence is privative,' or by what is derived from its opposite, like our saying: 'Existence is non-existent,' there is no impossibility in it. And what is here is of this kind, because we say: 'Existence is non-existent' or 'Existence is privative' meaning that it has no realization in the external, and it does not follow from that that the essence qualified by existence is non-existent, because non-existence is attached to its attribute—which is existence—not to the essence itself. And for this there are parallels in our speech. Do you not see that a human is characterized by standing and sitting, and whiteness and blackness, and the like. Then the human is a body and a substance, and everything by which a human is characterized is an accident, not a body nor a substance. So if you say: 'Zaid is standing' or 'Zaid is white,' you have described the body which is the human by something that is not a body. Thus in it there is describing the thing by its opposite, but because the meaning is that the human, who is a body, possesses something that is not a body, it is a valid meaning. And what is here is of this kind, for if you say: 'The essence is existent' along with our saying that existence is non-existent, the meaning becomes: the essence existing externally is qualified by something that does not exist externally, and there is nothing [objectionable] in this.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof from those who hold that existence is identical to the essence. They argue by reductio: if existence were additional to the essence, then that additional existence must itself either exist or not exist. If it exists, we ask about its existence, leading to infinite regress or circularity; if it does not exist, then we would describe existence as non-existent, a contradiction. The author then provides two answers from the opposing side. The first answer denies the exclusivity of the division, introducing a third category: the state (hal), which is neither existent nor non-existent. The second answer accepts that existence is non-existent but denies it is a contradiction, because describing something by its opposite is only impossible if it is the exact opposite, not a derived term. They give the example of describing a human, who is a body, by an accident (standing) which is not a body; this is not a contradiction because the attribute is not the essence. Similarly, saying 'existence is non-existent' means the attribute is non-existent, not the essence itself.

Key Terms
الوجود (al-wujud)
عين الموجود (ayn al-mawjud)
محال (muhaal)
نقيض (naqid)
دور (dawr)
تسلسل (tasalsul)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What are the two possibilities considered for the state of the additional existence?
A) Either it is necessary or contingent
B) Either it is existent or non-existent
C) Either it is eternal or created
D) Either it is substance or accident

Key Takeaway
The first proof argues that if existence were additional to essence, it would lead either to an infinite regress or a contradiction, forcing the conclusion that existence is identical to essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your very existence is not an added attribute but identical to your essence, how does that change the way you see your dependence on the One whose existence is necessary? Can you honestly say you live with that awareness today?
?
The proof shows that saying 'existence is non-existent' is a contradiction. But have you ever lived as though your existence were something you could take for granted, as if it were not constantly sustained? What practical shift would it take to acknowledge that your existence is not separate from the Essence of Allah?
?
If you accept that existence is identical to essence, then your existence is not merely something you 'have' but something you 'are'. Are you ready to let go of the illusion that your existence is an independent possession and surrender to the reality that you exist only through Him? What one step will you take today to live in that truth?

Allah's Existence
intermediate
7 min
First Refutation: Denying the Binary Division and Introducing 'Hal'
Presents the first refutation by denying binary division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first refutation of the proof, which denies the exclusive binary division and introduces 'hal' as a third category.

Core Idea
The division into existent and non-existent is not exhaustive; there is a third state: the 'hal'.

The first answer to the previous proof cuts directly at its core assumption: that everything must either exist or not exist. The proponent here introduces a third metaphysical category—the 'hal' (state)—which is neither existent nor non-existent. This challenges the very foundation of the reductio.

Guided Path
1
Read the first answer
Notice how the responder refuses to accept the binary division. They claim that the additional existence can be in a state called 'hal'.
The term 'hal' is technical in kalam, signifying something that is neither existent nor non-existent but an attribute inhering in an essence.
2
Understand the third category
The 'hal' is a state that is not independently existent but also not absolutely non-existent. It is the mode of being of an attribute attached to an essence.
3
See how this refutes the proof
If additional existence can be a 'hal', then neither branch of the proof applies. The regress and contradiction are avoided.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof from those who hold that existence is identical to the essence. They argue by reductio: if existence were additional to the essence, then that additional existence must itself either exist or not exist. If it exists, we ask about its existence, leading to infinite regress or circularity; if it does not exist, then we would describe existence as non-existent, a contradiction. The author then provides two answers from the opposing side. The first answer denies the exclusivity of the division, introducing a third category: the state (hal), which is neither existent nor non-existent. The second answer accepts that existence is non-existent but denies it is a contradiction, because describing something by its opposite is only impossible if it is the exact opposite, not a derived term. They give the example of describing a human, who is a body, by an accident (standing) which is not a body; this is not a contradiction because the attribute is not the essence. Similarly, saying 'existence is non-existent' means the attribute is non-existent, not the essence itself.

الحال (al-hal)
نقيض (naqid)
محال (muhaal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that every attribute of an essence must either exist independently or be nothing at all.
Correction: The passage introduces 'hal' as a middle category: attributes that are neither independently existent nor completely non-existent, but a state of the essence.

Checkpoint
What third category does the first refutation introduce to avoid the binary division?
A) The possible
B) The contingent
C) The state (hal)
D) The mental existent

Key Takeaway
The first refutation undermines the proof by denying its binary division and proposing a third category 'hal', which saves the possibility of existence being additional without logical absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the reality of your own qualities—like your knowledge or your faith—do you treat them as mere labels (non-existent) or as independent things (existent)? How does the concept of 'hal' challenge you to see them as real but not separate? In what area of your life are you denying the reality of something that is genuinely a part of you but not a separate thing? Will you start acknowledging it as a 'hal'—real and additional?
?
The refutation hinges on the claim that not everything is either existent or non-existent. Have you ever forced your understanding of God into a binary (either this or that) when a third option—one that preserves both mystery and reality—was available? What belief about Allah have you oversimplified? What would it mean to accept a 'hal' category in your own faith? Are you willing to hold the tension of something being real yet not fully comprehensible?
?
The 'hal' is a category that theologians debated seriously. When you face a difficult concept in aqeedah, do you dismiss it as a semantic trick, or do you engage with it as a genuine attempt to understand? What does your reaction say about your thirst for truth? Will you commit to wrestling with difficult theological ideas rather than settling for easy binaries?

advanced

Second Refutation: Accepting Non-Existence and Denying Contradiction
Presents the second refutation with an analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second refutation, which accepts that additional existence is non-existent but argues that this is not a contradiction.

Core Idea
Describing something by a derived opposite is not a contradiction; only the exact opposite is.

The second answer takes a different tack: it accepts that additional existence is non-existent, but denies that this leads to contradiction. By distinguishing between describing the essence itself and describing its attribute, the responder shows that saying 'existence is non-existent' is not the same as saying 'the essence is non-existent'. An analogy from everyday attributes illustrates the point.

Guided Path
1
Read the second answer
Note the concession: the responder accepts that additional existence is non-existent. Then they clarify the definition of impossibility.
Impossibility is only when you describe something by its exact opposite.
2
Understand the distinction
The responder distinguishes between saying 'existence is non-existence' (exact opposite, impossible) and 'existence is non-existent' (derived, possible). The latter means the existence-attribute has no external reality.
3
Examine the analogy
Just as we describe a human (a body) as 'standing' which is not a body, we are not contradicting ourselves. Similarly, we describe an essence as existent while its attribute (existence) is non-existent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد استدل القائلون بأن الوجود عين الموجود بحجج كثيرة نجتزئ لك منها بدليلين:

And those who say that existence is identical to the existent have argued with many proofs, of which we will present two for you: The first: They said: If existence were something additional to the existent, then the state of this additional existence would not be free from one of two matters: either it is existent, or it is non-existent. If it is existent, then the discussion moves to its existence, and so on; thus circularity or infinite regress follows, both of which are false. So what leads to that—namely, existence being an existent thing additional to the essence—is false. And if existence is non-existent, then it would entail describing the thing which is existence by its opposite, so it would be said: 'Existence is non-existent,' and that is impossible because it combines opposites. And this proof can be answered with one of two answers: The first of them: We do not accept the exclusive division in your statement: If this existence is additional to the essence, then its state is not free from either being existent or being non-existent. Rather, we claim that it is additional to the essence and yet it is neither existent nor non-existent, because the division is not binary according to them as you have learned; rather, there is a third division, which is: the state (hal). The second answer: We grant what you mentioned, but we choose the second branch, namely that it is non-existent, and we do not accept that on the assumption of its being non-existent it entails describing the thing by its opposite and that it is impossible. For impossibility is only describing the thing by its exact opposite, like our saying: 'Existence is non-existence' and our saying: 'The existent is non-existent.' As for describing the thing by what is related to its opposite, like our saying: 'Existence is privative,' or by what is derived from its opposite, like our saying: 'Existence is non-existent,' there is no impossibility in it. And what is here is of this kind, because we say: 'Existence is non-existent' or 'Existence is privative' meaning that it has no realization in the external, and it does not follow from that that the essence qualified by existence is non-existent, because non-existence is attached to its attribute—which is existence—not to the essence itself. And for this there are parallels in our speech. Do you not see that a human is characterized by standing and sitting, and whiteness and blackness, and the like. Then the human is a body and a substance, and everything by which a human is characterized is an accident, not a body nor a substance. So if you say: 'Zaid is standing' or 'Zaid is white,' you have described the body which is the human by something that is not a body. Thus in it there is describing the thing by its opposite, but because the meaning is that the human, who is a body, possesses something that is not a body, it is a valid meaning. And what is here is of this kind, for if you say: 'The essence is existent' along with our saying that existence is non-existent, the meaning becomes: the essence existing externally is qualified by something that does not exist externally, and there is nothing [objectionable] in this.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof from those who hold that existence is identical to the essence. They argue by reductio: if existence were additional to the essence, then that additional existence must itself either exist or not exist. If it exists, we ask about its existence, leading to infinite regress or circularity; if it does not exist, then we would describe existence as non-existent, a contradiction. The author then provides two answers from the opposing side. The first answer denies the exclusivity of the division, introducing a third category: the state (hal), which is neither existent nor non-existent. The second answer accepts that existence is non-existent but denies it is a contradiction, because describing something by its opposite is only impossible if it is the exact opposite, not a derived term. They give the example of describing a human, who is a body, by an accident (standing) which is not a body; this is not a contradiction because the attribute is not the essence. Similarly, saying 'existence is non-existent' means the attribute is non-existent, not the essence itself.

Key Terms
نقيض (naqid)
محال (muhaal)
عدمي (adami)
مشتق (mushtaq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if existence is non-existent, then the essence must also be non-existent.
Correction: Non-existence only attaches to the attribute (existence), not to the essence. The essence remains existent.

Checkpoint
What is the key distinction that allows the second refutation to avoid contradiction?
A) Existence is not an attribute
B) The non-existence attaches to the attribute, not the essence
C) The essence itself becomes non-existent
D) The binary division is still valid

Key Takeaway
The second refutation accepts that existence is non-existent but argues this is not a contradiction, because non-existence is at the level of the attribute, not the essence, as illustrated by the analogy with human attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe Allah as 'not being unjust,' are you careful to mean that injustice is absent from His actions, not that He possesses the opposite attribute? This lesson distinguishes exact opposite from derived opposite—how will you guard your tongue when speaking of Allah's attributes?
?
You have just learned a precise logical tool to avoid contradiction. But do you apply such rigor to your own beliefs? Reflect on a time you accused someone of contradiction without understanding the nuance. Will you now take the care to distinguish exact from derived opposites in your arguments?
?
This lesson shows that an attribute can be non-existent while the essence remains. Have you ever felt your own identity threatened by a temporary lack? How does knowing that non-existence can be on the attribute level free you from despair when you lack good deeds?

The First Proof and Its Two Answers
First proof against additional existence and two responses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first rational proof against the view that existence is an additional attribute to the essence, along with the two main kalam responses to that proof.

Core Idea
Existence cannot be an added attribute without leading to contradiction or infinite regress.

In this passage, Sanusi presents the first of several arguments against the view that existence is a separate attribute added to the essence. The argument is structured as a dilemma, and two responses are offered. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and clarifies the nuanced defenses, which revolve around the concept of 'states' (ahwal) and careful logical distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the full Arabic passage and its English translation to get a sense of the argument without analysis.
2
Understand the proof's dilemma
Identify the dilemma: if existence is an additional attribute, it must itself be either existent or non-existent. Each horn leads to a different problem (infinite regress / contradiction).
The proof relies on the law of excluded middle.
3
Examine the first answer: hal
The first response rejects the binary division and introduces 'haal' (state) as a third category that is neither existent nor non-existent. This is a distinctive Ash'ari doctrine.
This answer preserves the reality of the attribute without reifying it.
4
Examine the second answer: reinterpretation
The second response accepts that existence is non-existent but denies that this describes the essence as non-existent. It distinguishes between describing the attribute and the essence.
An analogy with accidents (e.g., whiteness) is used to illustrate.
5
Reflect on the analogy
Consider how describing a body (substance) with an accident (non-substance) is not contradictory, and how this parallels calling existence 'non-existent' while the essence remains existent.
This analogy is key to understanding the logical move.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأوَّل: قالوا لو كان الوجود أمراً زائداً على الموجود لكان حال هذا الوجود الزائد لا يخلو عن أحد أمرين : إما أن يكون موجوداً ، وإما أن يكون معدوماً ، فإن كان موجوداً انتقل الكلام إلى وجوده ، وهكذا ؛ فيلزم عليه الدور أو التسلسل وكلاهما باطل ، فما أدى إليه وهو كون الوجود أمراً موجوداً زائداً على الذات باطل، وإن كان الوجود معدوماً لزم اتصاف الشيء الذي هو الوجود بنقيضه فيقال: «الوجود معدوم» وذلك محال لأنه جمع بين النقيضين . ويمكن أن يجاب عن هذا الدليل بأحد جوابين: أولهما: أنا لا نسلم الحصر في قولكم: إن كان هذا الوجود زائدا على الذات فلا يخلو حاله إما أن يكون موجوداً وإما أن يكون معدوما بل ندعي أنه زائد على الذات ومع ذلك فليس موجوداً ولا معدوماً ، لأن القسمة ليست ثنائية عندهم على ما علمت ، بل هناك قسم ثالث وهو: الحال . والجواب الثاني: سلمنا أنه على ما ذكرتم، ولكنا نختار الشَّقَّ الثَّاني، وهو أنه معدوم، ولا نسلم أنه على فرض كونه معدوماً يستلزم وصف الشيء بنقيضه وأنه محال ، لأن المحال إنَّما وصف الشَّيء بعين نقيضه كقولنا : الوجود عدم وقولنا : الموجود معدوم» ، وأما وصف الشيء بالمنسوب إلى نقيضه، كقولنا: الوجود عدمي»، أو بالمشتق من نقيضه كقولنا: الوجود معدوم فلا استحالة فيه، وما هنا من هذا القبيل ؛ لأنَّنا نقول: «الوجود معدوم» أو «الوجود عدمي» على معنى أنه لا تحقق له في الخارج، ولا يلزمُ مِن ذلك أنْ تكونَ الذَّات المتصفة بالوجود معدومةً ، لأنَّ العدم قائمٌ بوصفها - وهو الوجود ـ لا بنفس الذات ، ولهذا نظائر في كلامنا، ألا ترى أنَّ الإنسان يتصف بالقيام والقعود، والبياض والسواد، ونحو ذلك . ثمَّ إن الإنسان جسمٌ وجوهرٌ ، وكل ما يتصف الإنسان به عرض ليس بجسم ولا بجوهر فإذا قلت: زيد قائم أو زيد أبيض فقد وصفت الجسم الذي هو الإنسان بما ليس بجسم، ففيه وصف الشَّيء بنقيضه، لكن لما كان المعنى على أنَّ الإنسان الذي هو جسم صاحب شيئا ليس بجسم كان معنى صحيحاً ، وما هنا من هذا القبيل، فإنك لو قلت: «الذات موجودة مع قولنا بأن الوجود معدوم يصير المعنى الذاتُ الموجودة خارجا متصفةٌ بشيء ليس موجودا في الخارج، ولا شيء في هذا.
The first [proof]: They said: If existence were an additional matter to the existent, then the state of this additional existence would not be free from one of two things: either it is existent, or it is non-existent. If it is existent, the discussion moves to its existence, and so on; thus there would follow either circularity or an infinite regress, both of which are invalid. So what leads to it—which is that existence is an existent matter additional to the essence—is invalid. And if existence were non-existent, then it would necessitate describing the thing which is existence with its opposite, so it would be said: 'Existence is non-existent,' and that is impossible because it combines the two opposites. And it is possible to answer this proof with one of two answers: The first: We do not accept the restriction in your statement: 'If this existence is additional to the essence, then its state is not free from being either existent or non-existent.' Rather, we claim that it is additional to the essence and yet it is neither existent nor non-existent, because the division is not binary according to them, as you have learned; rather, there is a third division, which is the state (al-haal). The second answer: We grant that it is as you mentioned, but we choose the second alternative, which is that it is non-existent, and we do not accept that on the assumption of its being non-existent it necessitates describing a thing with its opposite and that this is impossible. For the impossible is only describing a thing with the exact opposite of itself, like our saying: 'Existence is non-existence' and our saying: 'The existent is non-existent.' As for describing a thing with what is attributed to its opposite, like our saying: 'Existence is privative (adamiyy),' or with a derivative of its opposite like our saying: 'Existence is non-existent (ma'dum),' there is no impossibility in it. What is here is of this kind, because we say: 'Existence is non-existent' or 'Existence is privative' meaning that it has no realization in the external, and from that it does not necessarily follow that the essence described by existence is non-existent, because non-existence subsists in its attribute—which is existence—not in the essence itself. And there are analogies for this in our speech. Do you not see that a human is described by standing and sitting, whiteness and blackness, and the like. Then the human is a body and a substance, and everything that a human is described by is an accident, not a body nor a substance. So if you say: 'Zaid is standing' or 'Zaid is white,' you have described the body which is the human with what is not a body, so therein is describing a thing with its opposite. But because the meaning is that the human who is a body possesses something not a body, the meaning is sound. What is here is of this kind, for if you say: 'The essence is existent' while we say that existence is non-existent, the meaning becomes: the essence existing externally is described by something not existing externally, and there is nothing in this.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof against the view that existence is an additional attribute to the essence, along with two answers. The argument is structured as a dilemma: if existence is additional, it must either itself be existent or non-existent. If existent, the same question applies to its existence ad infinitum, leading to circularity or infinite regress, both invalid. If non-existent, then we describe existence—which is supposed to be a positive attribute—with non-existence, a contradiction. The author then gives two responses. The first answer denies the binary division, introducing the concept of 'haal' (state) as a third category that is neither existent nor non-existent. This reflects the Ash'ari position that some attributes (ahwal) are not independently existent but are real states. The second answer accepts the second horn but denies the impossibility: describing existence as 'non-existent' is not describing the essence as non-existent; it is merely saying that existence has no external reality (like an accident) while the essence itself is existent. This is compared to describing a body (substance) with an accident (e.g., standing), which is not a substance. The analogy shows that attributing a non-existent quality to an existent subject is not contradictory.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The second answer simply grants that existence is non-existent, which would imply the essence is also non-existent.
Correction: The second answer distinguishes between the attribute (existence) and the essence. It is the attribute that is described as non-existent, not the essence itself. The essence remains existent.

Checkpoint

A) Existent
B) Non-existent
C) State (haal)
D) Essence

Key Takeaway
The first proof against existence as an additional attribute is answered either by denying the binary division (introducing hal) or by reinterpreting 'non-existent' as a description of the attribute, not the essence, using an analogy with accidents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument shows that existence cannot be an added attribute without contradiction. When you reflect on your own existence, do you see it as something separable from your essence, or as integral to who you are? How does this shape your gratitude to Allah for creating you?
?
The second answer distinguishes between describing the attribute (existence) as non-existent and describing the essence as non-existent. In your daily life, do you ever confuse your attributes (e.g., your actions, your thoughts) with your very self, leading to despair or pride? How can understanding this distinction help you repent and turn to Allah?

The Second Proof on Existence and Its Answer
Proof and answer on essence and existence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof against existence being additional to essence and the answer that distinguishes being a substrate for existence from being non-existent.

Core Idea
Being a substrate for existence does not make an essence non-existent.

The previous lesson introduced the first proof. Now Sanusi presents a second, more pointed proof and its powerful answer. This passage forces us to refine our understanding of what it means for an essence to be 'without existence'—it does not automatically mean non-existence. The example of the possible thing clarifies this distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the second proof as presented by Sanusi. Notice how it argues that if existence were additional, the essence without it would be non-existent, leading to a contradiction when we call it existent.
This proof is a reductio ad absurdum.
2
Identify the answer's key move
The answer denies the claim that being a substrate for existence implies non-existence. Find the exact phrasing of the denial.
3
Understand the example of the possible
Sanusi uses the possible (mumkin) as an example. Reflect on how the possible thing in itself is neither existent nor non-existent, but merely apt for both. How does this refute the proof?
The possible is not non-existent; it is a potentiality.
4
Connect to the broader theology
Consider why this distinction matters for the doctrine of divine simplicity and the nature of existence in Allah. The answer preserves the doctrine that existence is additional without implying non-existence of the essence.
This is a subtle but crucial point in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدليل الثاني: قالوا: لو كان الوجود أمراً زائداً على الذات عارضا لها ؛ لكانت الذات من حيث هي - أي: مع قطع النظر عن هذا الوجود ـ غير موجودة، على معنى أنها حينئذ تكون معروضة للوجود صالحة له خالية عنه ، فتكون معدومةً فإذا قلت : «الذات موجودة» كنت قد وصفت المعدومَ بالوجودِ وهذا تناقض . ويجاب عن هذا: أنا لا نسلم أنَّ الذات إذا كانت معروضة للوجود صالحة له خالية عنه تكون معدومة ؛ لأن الممكن مثلا : هو ما لا تقتضي ذاته أن يكون موجوداً أو معدوما فهو صالح لأن يتوارد عليه الوجود والعدم على سبيل البدل ، فهو من حيث ذاته عار عنهما جميعاً ، نعني أنَّ ماهيته في حد ذاتها خالية عنهما غير موصوفة بواحد منهما.
The second proof: They said: If existence were an additional matter to the essence, accidental to it, then the essence in itself—i.e., ignoring this existence—would be non-existent, meaning that it would be a substrate for existence, apt for it, devoid of it, so it would be non-existent. Then if you say: 'The essence is existent,' you would have described the non-existent with existence, and this is a contradiction. And it is answered: We do not accept that when the essence is a substrate for existence, apt for it, devoid of it, it is non-existent; because the possible, for example, is that whose essence does not necessitate that it be existent or non-existent; it is apt for existence and non-existence to come upon it alternately, so in itself it is devoid of both, meaning that its quiddity in its own essence is free from both, not described by either.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof against existence being additional and its answer. The proof argues that if existence is an accident added to the essence, then the essence without that accident would be non-existent; but we describe it as existent, which is contradictory. The answer denies the premise: being a substrate for existence does not mean the essence is non-existent. The example of the possible (mumkin) illustrates this: a possible thing in itself is neither existent nor non-existent; it is just apt for both. Similarly, the essence of the Necessary Being, if existence were additional, would be a substrate for existence but not non-existent. The answer thus separates the state of being a substrate from actual non-existence.

Key Terms

ذات
معدوم
ممكن
ماهية

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That if an essence is without existence, it must be non-existent.
Correction: The example of the possible shows that an essence can be devoid of existence without being non-existent; it is merely a substrate apt for existence.

Checkpoint

A) The impossible
B) The necessary
C) The possible
D) The accidental

Key Takeaway
The second proof fails because being a substrate for existence does not entail non-existence; the possible thing is neither existent nor non-existent in its essence.

?

The Third Proof: Known Existence vs. Unknown Essence
The author presents a proof for the distinction between essence and existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the proof that distinguishes God's existence from His essence based on what is known to us.

Core Idea
The proof claims we know God's existence but not His essence, so they must be different.

This lesson presents a key argument used by those who hold that existence is additional to essence. The argument is a simple syllogism: what is known is different from what is unknown. Since we know God's existence but not His essence, the two must be distinct. See the argument in the author's own words.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The passage begins with an introduction to the proof.
2
Identify the syllogism
The author explicitly calls it a 'second-figure syllogism'. Identify the two premises and the conclusion.
Premise 1: Essence is unknown. Premise 2: Existence is known. Conclusion: They are different.
3
Reflect on the logical form
Second-figure syllogisms have a specific pattern. Consider whether this syllogism is valid and why it might be considered a proof.
Validity is not the same as soundness; the refutation will challenge the truth of the premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد استدل القائلون بأنَّ الوجود أمر زائد على الذات بأدلة نذكر لك منها واحدا، لتعرف مهيع البحث. قالوا: إنَّ ذاته تعالى غير معلومة لنا، وإنَّ وجوده سبحانه معلوم لنا، وهذا قياس من الشكل الثاني ونتيجته: «إن ذاته تعالى غير وجوده». ويجاب عن هذا الدليل: بأنا لا نُسلّم إطلاق قولكم: إن وجوده تعالى معلوم لنا لأنَّ حقيقة الوجود غير معلومة لنا ، كما أنَّ حقيقة الذات غير معلومة لنا، وإنما المعلوم لنا: الوجودُ مِن حيث الوصف، وهو أنَّ ذلك الوجودَ لا يسبقه عدمٌ ، ولا يلحقه عدم، ولو قلنا: إنَّ ذاته أيضا معلومة لنا من حيث الوصف ؛ ككونها لا تُشبه شيئًا ولا يشبهها شيء كان صحيحاً أيضاً.
And those who say that existence is additional to the essence have adduced proofs; we mention to you one of them so that you may know the path of inquiry. They said: His essence, exalted, is unknown to us, while His existence, glorified, is known to us. This is a second-figure syllogism whose conclusion is: 'His essence, exalted, is other than His existence.' And this proof is answered: We do not accept the unqualified statement that His existence, exalted, is known to us, because the reality of existence is unknown to us, just as the reality of the essence is unknown to us. Rather, what is known to us is existence in terms of description, i.e., that that existence is preceded by no non-existence and followed by no non-existence. And if we said that His essence is also known to us in terms of description, such as that it resembles nothing and nothing resembles it, that would also be correct.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage presents a third proof for the distinction between essence and existence, along with its refutation. The proof is a second-figure syllogism: we know God's existence but not His essence, so they must be different. The answer rejects the major premise: we do not know the reality (haqiqa) of existence any more than we know the reality of the essence. What we know are merely descriptive attributes (al-wasf) – for existence, that it is eternal (no prior or subsequent non-existence); for essence, that it is incomparable. So the proof fails because both are known only through attributes, not in themselves.

Key Terms
ذات
وجود
معلوم
قياس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion of the syllogism presented in this passage?
A) Existence is unknown
B) Essence is known
C) Essence is other than existence
D) The proof is invalid

Key Takeaway
The proof relies on the supposed knowability of existence versus unknowability of essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that God exists, but do you truly live as if His essence is beyond your comprehension? How does this change the way you approach Him in prayer—are you content with not fully understanding Him?
?
The proof says His essence is unknown. Are you secretly trying to grasp God completely with your mind, or do you humbly accept the limit of your knowledge? How does this lack of full knowledge impact your trust in Him?
?
If existence is known but essence unknown, your relationship with God is based on signs, not essence. Are you truly attentive to His signs in your daily life, or do you ignore them while claiming to believe in His existence?

Refutation: We Know Neither in Reality
The author refutes the proof by denying knowledge of the reality of existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the refutation of the proof: we know existence only through description, not in its reality.

Core Idea
The refutation denies that we know the reality of existence, only its descriptive attributes.

The previous lesson presented a proof that distinguished essence from existence. Now we see how the author refutes it: the claim that existence is known is rejected because we do not know the reality of existence, only its description. The same applies to essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the answer passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the key terms 'haqiqa' (reality) and 'wasf' (description).
The answer starts with 'We do not accept the unqualified statement...'
2
Identify the refutation strategy
The refutation denies the major premise that existence is known. It argues that the 'reality' of existence is unknown, just like the reality of essence.
This is a direct attack on the premise, not the logic.
3
Understand what is actually known
The author says we know existence only 'min haythu al-wasf' (in terms of description). Specifically, we know it as eternal (no prior or subsequent non-existence).
Similarly, we can know essence through its description of incomparability.
4
Appreciate the parallel treatment
The answer shows that both essence and existence are only known through attributes, so the asymmetry in the proof collapses.
The proof required one to be known and the other unknown; here both are known in the same way.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد استدل القائلون بأنَّ الوجود أمر زائد على الذات بأدلة نذكر لك منها واحدا، لتعرف مهيع البحث. قالوا: إنَّ ذاته تعالى غير معلومة لنا، وإنَّ وجوده سبحانه معلوم لنا، وهذا قياس من الشكل الثاني ونتيجته: «إن ذاته تعالى غير وجوده». ويجاب عن هذا الدليل: بأنا لا نُسلّم إطلاق قولكم: إن وجوده تعالى معلوم لنا لأنَّ حقيقة الوجود غير معلومة لنا ، كما أنَّ حقيقة الذات غير معلومة لنا، وإنما المعلوم لنا: الوجودُ مِن حيث الوصف، وهو أنَّ ذلك الوجودَ لا يسبقه عدمٌ ، ولا يلحقه عدم، ولو قلنا: إنَّ ذاته أيضا معلومة لنا من حيث الوصف ؛ ككونها لا تُشبه شيئًا ولا يشبهها شيء كان صحيحاً أيضاً.
And those who say that existence is additional to the essence have adduced proofs; we mention to you one of them so that you may know the path of inquiry. They said: His essence, exalted, is unknown to us, while His existence, glorified, is known to us. This is a second-figure syllogism whose conclusion is: 'His essence, exalted, is other than His existence.' And this proof is answered: We do not accept the unqualified statement that His existence, exalted, is known to us, because the reality of existence is unknown to us, just as the reality of the essence is unknown to us. Rather, what is known to us is existence in terms of description, i.e., that that existence is preceded by no non-existence and followed by no non-existence. And if we said that His essence is also known to us in terms of description, such as that it resembles nothing and nothing resembles it, that would also be correct.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage presents a third proof for the distinction between essence and existence, along with its refutation. The proof is a second-figure syllogism: we know God's existence but not His essence, so they must be different. The answer rejects the major premise: we do not know the reality (haqiqa) of existence any more than we know the reality of the essence. What we know are merely descriptive attributes (al-wasf) – for existence, that it is eternal (no prior or subsequent non-existence); for essence, that it is incomparable. So the proof fails because both are known only through attributes, not in themselves.

وصف
عدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The answer concedes that we know existence but not essence.
Correction: The answer says we know neither in reality; we only know both through descriptive attributes.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, what do we know about God's existence?
A) Its reality
B) Its descriptive attribute of eternality
C) Its essence
D) Nothing at all

Key Takeaway
The refutation shows that both essence and existence are known only through attributes, not in themselves.

Three Views on Existence: Philosophers, Karramiyya, and Ash'aris
Summarizes three views on existence and the Ash'ari definition with figurative nuance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different theological positions on existence (philosophers, Karramiyya, Ash'ari) and why the Ash'ari definition is considered figurative.

Core Idea
Existence is not a real attribute; it's a self-subsistent description.

The nature of existence was debated among theologians. This passage presents three key positions: the philosophers' view that existence is identical to God's essence, the Karramiyya's view that it's a real attribute, and the Ash'ari definition which treats existence as a self-subsistent attribute but only figuratively. Understanding this clarifies classical Islamic theology's nuanced approach.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the three distinct sections: philosophers, Karramiyya, Ash'aris.
The passage ends abruptly due to OCR; focus on the complete ideas presented.

Identify the three positions
List the philosophers' view (existence = essence for God), the Karramiyya view (real attribute), and the Ash'ari definition (self-subsistent attribute).
Each group uses different technical language.
3
Examine the Ash'ari figurative usage
The author notes that calling existence an 'attribute' is figurative (tajawuz) or lenient (tasamuh). Understand why: it's like a real attribute only in linguistic predication.
This corrects a common misunderstanding.
4
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to confirm you can distinguish the Ash'ari definition.
Refer only to this passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا، وقد قالت الفلاسفة : إنّ الوجود غير الموجود في الحادث، وأما في القديم فوجوده عينه ، لأنه تعالى واجب الوجودِ وهو واحدٌ من كل وجه ، فلو كان وجوده غيره لتكثر ، لأن الموصوف يتكثّر عندهم بكثرة الصفات، وذلك يؤدي إلى التركيب المؤدي إلى الإمكان، وهو مناف للوجوب ، ورَدُّ ذلك غير خاف. وقالت الكرَّاميّة : الوجود صفة معنى كالقدرة والإرادة. إذا علمت ذلك فاعلم أن الأشاعرة يُعرفونَ الوجودَ بأنه: «صفةٌ نفسية يدلُّ الوصف بها على الذات دون معنی زائد عليها» ، وقد يقال: «الوجود صفة تصحح لموصوفها أن يُرى»، وهذا هو الذي ذكره الشارح الله ، وقد علمت أن تعبيرهم عن الوجود بأنه صفة على مذهب الأشعري فيه تسامح ، والمراد أنه تجوز حيثُ شبَّهوا الوجود بالصفة الحقيقية كالعلم، بجامع أنَّ كلا منهما يقع صفةٌ في اللفظ ، فيقال: «ذات الله موجودة كما يقال: «الله عالم» واستعاروا=
This, and the philosophers said: Existence is other than the existent in the contingent, but in the Eternal, His existence is identical to Him, because He, exalted, is Necessary of Existence and is one from every aspect. If His existence were other than Him, multiplicity would occur, because the described becomes multiple according to them by multiplicity of attributes, and that leads to composition leading to possibility, which contradicts necessity. And its refutation is not hidden. And the Karramiyya said: Existence is an attribute of meaning (sifa ma'na) like power and will. When you know that, know that the Ash'aris define existence as: 'A self-subsistent attribute (sifa nafsiyya) whose description indicates the essence without additional meaning beyond it.' And it is said: 'Existence is an attribute that renders its described object visible.' This is what the commentator (rahimahu Allah) mentioned. And you have known that their expression of existence as an attribute, according to the Ash'ari school, involves latitude (tasamuh); the intended meaning is that they used figurative speech (tajawuz) by likening existence to a real attribute like knowledge, under the common factor that each of them occurs as an attribute in speech, so it is said: 'The essence of God is existent' just as it is said: 'God is knowing,' and they borrowed...
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage presents the views of philosophers, the Karramiyya, and the Ash'ari definition of existence. The philosophers hold that for contingent beings existence is other than the existent, but for God existence is identical to His essence, otherwise there would be multiplicity and composition, implying contingency. The Karramiyya consider existence a real attribute (sifa ma'na) like power. The Ash'aris define it as a 'sifa nafsiyya' (self-subsistent attribute) that merely indicates the essence without adding a meaning. Some also define it as that which makes the subject visible (in the afterlife). The author notes that calling existence an attribute is a figurative usage (tajawuz) or lenient expression (tasamuh), because they liken it to a real attribute like knowledge in terms of linguistic predication, but it is not a real additional attribute. The passage ends abruptly with 'and they borrowed...' indicating continuation.

Correction: The author explicitly states that calling existence an 'attribute' is figurative (tajawuz) or lenient (tasamuh); it is likened to a real attribute only in linguistic predication, not ontologically.

The Meaning of Wajib al-Wujud
Sanusi defines wajib al-wujud as the impossibility of non-existence at all times.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of wajib al-wujud (Necessary of Existence) for God as the absolute impossibility of non-existence in all times.

Core Idea
God's existence is necessary because non-existence is impossible for Him at any time.

This lesson begins a new section on the divine attribute of wajib al-wujud. The author defines it clearly: God's existence is so necessary that non-existence cannot be imagined for Him, whether in the eternal past or the eternal future. This forms the foundation for understanding God's uniqueness in existence.

1

The meaning of wajib al-wujud is that non-existence is not permissible for God at any time. This means non-existence is impossible—both logically and existentially.
This is a negative definition: it tells us what God is not (non-existent) rather than what He is.

The Meaning of God's Necessary Existence
Definition of necessary existence for God

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what it means for God to have necessary existence, including that non-existence is impossible for Him in both the eternal past and future, and that this is grounded in His essence being self-sufficient.

Core Idea
God's necessary existence means He never was absent and never will be.

This lesson defines the foundational concept of wājib al-wujūd—necessary existence—as applied to God. It clarifies that not only does God always exist now, but non-existence is categorically impossible for Him in any timeframe, past or future. The reason for this impossibility is rooted in the nature of God's existence itself, not in any external cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage uses two time terms: azalan (eternally past) and abadan (everlastingly future).
2
Identify the two temporal directions
The passage denies non-existence to God in two directions: a primordial past (azalan) and an unending future (abadan). This means God never began and never ceases.
3
Examine the reason given
The passage gives three tightly connected reasons: existence is from His essence, nothing else caused it, and what is essential cannot fail. Reflect on why these make non-existence impossible.
This is the core of the definition—necessary existence is not arbitrary but logically grounded.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما معنى وجوب الوجود له سبحانه، فإنه لا يجوز عليه سبحانه العدم لا أزلا ولا أبدا، على معنى أنه لا يجوز الحكم عليه بالعدم سواء أكان العدم أزلاً أم أبدا، وذلك بسبب أن وجوده لذاته، وأن غيره لم يؤثر فيه، وما بالذات لا يتخلف.
And as for the meaning of the necessity of existence for Him, exalted is He, it is that non-existence is not permissible for Him, neither eternally nor everlastingly, in the sense that it is not permissible to judge Him with non-existence, whether the non-existence is eternal or everlasting. And that is because His existence is from His essence, and that other than Him has not effected it, and what is from the essence does not lag behind.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage defines the meaning of 'necessary existence' (wājib al-wujūd) for God. It is a definition passage. It states that non-existence (al-ʿadam) is impossible for God both in the eternal past (azalan) and in the eternal future (abadan). The phrase 'neither eternally nor everlastingly' covers both temporal directions. The reason given is threefold: (1) His existence is from His essence (lī dhātihi), meaning He does not derive existence from another; (2) nothing other than Him has effected (aththara) His existence; and (3) whatever is essential (mā bi al-dhāt) does not fail to occur (lā yatakhallaf). Thus, necessary existence is not just a label but is grounded in self-sufficiency and essentiality.

Correction: Necessary existence means non-existence is impossible in both directions of time, and it is grounded in essential self-sufficiency.

The Syllogism and the Minor Premise
Sanusi presents the syllogism and justifies the minor premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the kalam syllogism proving God's necessary existence and the evidence that the world is originated and depends on God.

Core Idea
The proof that God is necessary is a simple syllogism: the world depends on God, and whatever depends on God is necessary.

This lesson introduces the first part of Imam al-Sanusi's proof that God is necessary of existence. The proof is presented as a syllogism, which is the standard logical form in kalam. Here, we focus on the syllogism itself and the evidence for the minor premise, which is the world's originatedness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the translation.
2
Identify the syllogism parts
Locate the two premises and the conclusion. Notice the wording: minor premise says the world depends on God, major says whoever is depended on is necessary of existence.
The syllogism is given in the first two lines.
3
Examine the minor premise evidence
The evidence for the minor premise is that the world is originated (ḥādith) and every originated thing depends on an originator (muḥdith). This is a standard kalam argument.
This premise is already established earlier in the course.
4
Reflect on the argument's validity
Consider why this syllogism logically leads to the conclusion. The conclusion is that God is necessary of existence.
This sets up the next lesson on the major premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الدليل على وجوب الوجود له فنَظْمُهُ أن نقول: «الله تعالى يجب افتقار العالم كله إليه، وكل من وجب افتقار العالم إليه فهو واجب الوجود» وينتج هذا قولنا: الله تعالى واجب الوجود. أما المقدمة الأولى - وهي صغرى هذا القياس - فدليلها ما تقدم من أن العالم حادث، وكل حادث يجب افتقاره إلى محدث، وأما المقدمة الثانية - وهي كبرى القياس - فدليلها: أنه لو لم يكن واجب الوجود لكان جائز الوجود، ولو كان جائز الوجود لاحتاج إلى محدث يحدثه، ثم محدثه كذلك، فإن استمر الحال هكذا إلى ما لا نهاية لزم التسلسل، وإن رجع الأمر إلى الأول لزم الدور، وكل من الدور والتسلسل محال، فما أدى إليهما: وهو احتياجه سبحانه إلى محدث محال، فما أدى إليه وهو كونه جائز الوجود محال، فثبت نقيضه وهو كونه واجب الوجود.
And as for the proof of the necessity of existence for Him, its structure is that we say: 'God Almighty, the entire world must be dependent on Him, and everyone to whom the world must be dependent is necessary of existence.' And this yields our statement: 'God Almighty is necessary of existence.' As for the first premise — which is the minor of this syllogism — its evidence is what preceded: that the world is originated (ḥādith), and every originated thing must depend on an originator (muḥdith). As for the second premise — which is the major of the syllogism — its evidence is: if He were not necessary of existence, He would be possible of existence, and if He were possible of existence, He would need an originator to originate Him, then that originator likewise. If the situation continues thus ad infinitum, infinite regress (al-tasalsul) would follow; and if the matter goes back to the first, circularity (al-dawr) would follow. Both circularity and infinite regress are impossible. Therefore, what leads to them — namely His needing an originator — is impossible. Therefore, what leads to it — namely His being possible of existence — is impossible. Thus, its opposite is established, namely His being necessary of existence.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage presents a logical proof (dalīl) that God is necessary of existence (wājib al-wujūd). It is a proof passage structured as a syllogism. The syllogism has two premises: (minor) the entire world is dependent on God, and (major) anyone to whom the world is dependent is necessary of existence. The conclusion is that God is necessary of existence. The evidence for the minor premise is that the world is originated (ḥādith) and every originated thing needs an originator. The evidence for the major premise is a reductio ad absurdum: if God were not necessary, He would be possible (jā'iz al-wujūd). A possible existence needs an originator. That originator would itself either need an originator, leading to infinite regress (tasalsul), or if we trace back to the first, it would be circular (dawr). Both infinite regress and circularity are impossible. Therefore, the possibility of God being possible is eliminated, and the opposite—necessity—is established. This is a classic kalam argument for God's necessary existence, avoiding regress and circularity.

Key Terms
واجب الوجود
القياس
صغرى
كبرى
حادث
محدث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The world is originated
B) The world depends on God
C) Every originated thing needs an originator
D) God is necessary

Key Takeaway
The proof that God is necessary of existence begins with a valid syllogism: the world depends on God, and whatever depends on God is necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

6 min
The Major Premise and the Reductio Against Contingency
Sanusi uses a reductio argument to justify the major premise, proving God is necessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the justification for the major premise of the syllogism, which uses a reductio ad absurdum to eliminate contingency and infinite regress, thereby proving God's necessary existence.

Core Idea
If God were contingent, He would need an originator, leading to infinite regress or circularity—both impossible.

This lesson completes the proof of God's necessary existence by examining the evidence for the major premise of the syllogism. Al-Sanusi uses a reductio argument: if God were not necessary, He would be possible (jā'iz al-wujūd), and a possible being requires an originator. That originator would either require another originator, leading to infinite regress (tasalsul), or if we stop at the first, it would be circular (dawr). Since both are impossible, the original assumption of contingency is false, so God must be necessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the major premise
Read the Arabic passage focusing on the argument for the major premise. Notice its logical structure.
2
Understand the reductio assumption
The argument begins by assuming the opposite: that God is not necessary but possible. Trace the consequences of that assumption.
Possible means it can exist or not exist; it needs a cause.
3
Identify the two impossible outcomes
The assumption leads to either infinite regress (a chain with no end) or circularity (going back to the first). Both are impossible in kalam reasoning.
These are the key terms: al-tasalsul and al-dawr.
4
Conclude the necessity
Since the assumption leads to impossibility, its opposite must hold: God is necessary of existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الدليل على وجوب الوجود له فنَظْمُهُ أن نقول: «الله تعالى يجب افتقار العالم كله إليه، وكل من وجب افتقار العالم إليه فهو واجب الوجود» وينتج هذا قولنا: الله تعالى واجب الوجود. أما المقدمة الأولى - وهي صغرى هذا القياس - فدليلها ما تقدم من أن العالم حادث، وكل حادث يجب افتقاره إلى محدث، وأما المقدمة الثانية - وهي كبرى القياس - فدليلها: أنه لو لم يكن واجب الوجود لكان جائز الوجود، ولو كان جائز الوجود لاحتاج إلى محدث يحدثه، ثم محدثه كذلك، فإن استمر الحال هكذا إلى ما لا نهاية لزم التسلسل، وإن رجع الأمر إلى الأول لزم الدور، وكل من الدور والتسلسل محال، فما أدى إليهما: وهو احتياجه سبحانه إلى محدث محال، فما أدى إليه وهو كونه جائز الوجود محال، فثبت نقيضه وهو كونه واجب الوجود.
And as for the proof of the necessity of existence for Him, its structure is that we say: 'God Almighty, the entire world must be dependent on Him, and everyone to whom the world must be dependent is necessary of existence.' And this yields our statement: 'God Almighty is necessary of existence.' As for the first premise — which is the minor of this syllogism — its evidence is what preceded: that the world is originated (ḥādith), and every originated thing must depend on an originator (muḥdith). As for the second premise — which is the major of the syllogism — its evidence is: if He were not necessary of existence, He would be possible of existence, and if He were possible of existence, He would need an originator to originate Him, then that originator likewise. If the situation continues thus ad infinitum, infinite regress (al-tasalsul) would follow; and if the matter goes back to the first, circularity (al-dawr) would follow. Both circularity and infinite regress are impossible. Therefore, what leads to them — namely His needing an originator — is impossible. Therefore, what leads to it — namely His being possible of existence — is impossible. Thus, its opposite is established, namely His being necessary of existence.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage presents a logical proof (dalīl) that God is necessary of existence (wājib al-wujūd). It is a proof passage structured as a syllogism. The syllogism has two premises: (minor) the entire world is dependent on God, and (major) anyone to whom the world is dependent is necessary of existence. The conclusion is that God is necessary of existence. The evidence for the minor premise is that the world is originated (ḥādith) and every originated thing needs an originator. The evidence for the major premise is a reductio ad absurdum: if God were not necessary, He would be possible (jā'iz al-wujūd). A possible existence needs an originator. That originator would itself either need an originator, leading to infinite regress (tasalsul), or if we trace back to the first, it would be circular (dawr). Both infinite regress and circularity are impossible. Therefore, the possibility of God being possible is eliminated, and the opposite—necessity—is established. This is a classic kalam argument for God's necessary existence, avoiding regress and circularity.

Key Terms

جائز الوجود
كبرى
التسلسل
الدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God is possible, He could exist without a cause, like a self-caused being.
Correction: In kalam, everything that is possible (not necessary) requires a cause external to itself. If God were possible, He would need a cause, leading to an absurd chain.

Checkpoint
What does the argument conclude if assuming God is possible leads to an infinite regress or circularity?
A) Infinite regress is possible
B) God must be necessary
C) God can be both necessary and possible
D) The regress is acceptable

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of infinite regress and circularity proves that God cannot be contingent; He must be necessary of existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever lived as if your existence is self-sufficient, forgetting that every moment you depend on the Necessary Being? What would change if you truly believed that your very existence is contingent every second?
?
If God is not necessary in your life—if He is optional—then you have built your life on a foundation that can collapse. Is God necessary for you, or have you reduced Him to a mere possibility?
?
The proof shows that infinite regress is impossible. In your own life, are you chasing an endless chain of goals and desires without anchoring them in the Necessary? What would it mean to stop the regress and rest in the only Necessary Being?

The Syllogism for God's Necessary Existence
Sanusi presents the syllogism proving God's necessary existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical syllogism Sanusi uses to prove that God is necessarily existent (wajib al-wujud).

Core Idea
The world's contingency forces a self-necessary origin, not an endless chain.

In this lesson, Sanusi lays out a concise logical proof for God's necessary existence. Using a syllogism with two premises, he shows that the world, being originated, must depend on an originator, and that originator must be necessarily existent—otherwise we fall into the absurdities of infinite regress or circularity. This proof is foundational in kalam cosmology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get a feel for the argument. Notice the structure: a syllogism with a minor and major premise.
The passage is dense but logically tight.
2
Understand the first premise (minor)
Sanusi first says the world depends on God. This is because the world is originated (hadith) and needs an originator (muhdith). Recognize this as the cosmological argument from contingency.
The world's being 'hadith' means it came into existence after not existing.
3
Understand the second premise (major)
The second premise says that the one the world depends on must be necessarily existent. The evidence: if not necessary, He would be contingent, needing another originator, leading to infinite regress or circularity, both impossible. So He must be necessary.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The syllogism concludes that God, the world's originator, is necessarily existent. Reflect on how the argument rules out both regress and circularity.
5
Test your understanding with the checkpoint
Answer the multiple-choice question to ensure you grasp the structure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الدليل على وجوب الوجود له فَنَظْمُهُ أن نقول: «الله تعالى يجب افتقار العالم كله إليه، وكل من وجب افتقار العالم إليه فهو واجب الوجود وينتج هذا قولنا: الله تعالى واجب الوجود. أما المقدمة الأولى - وهي صغرى هذا القياس ـ فدليلها ما تقدم من أن العالم حادث، وكل حادث يجب افتقاره إلى محدث ، وأما المقدمة الثانية - وهي كبرى القياس - فدليلها : أنه لو لم يكن واجب الوجودِ لكانَ جائز الوجودِ ، ولو كان جائز الوجودِ لاحتاج إلى مُحدِث يحدِثُه ، ثم محدِثُه كذلك، فإن استمر الحال هكذا إلى ما لا نهاية لزِم التّسلسل ، وإنْ رجعَ الأمرُ إلى الأول لزم الدور، وكل من الدور والتسلسل محال، فما أدى إليهما: وهو احتياجه سبحانه إلى محدث محال، فما أدى إليه وهو كونه جائز الوجود محال، فثبت نقيضه وهو كونه واجب الوجود.
As for the proof for the necessity of existence for Him, its formulation is that we say: 'God Almighty, the entire world must be in need of Him, and everyone to whom the world's need is necessitated is necessarily existent (wajib al-wujud).' This produces our statement: 'God Almighty is necessarily existent.' As for the first premise—which is the minor of this syllogism—its evidence is what preceded, that the world is originated (hadith), and every originated thing must be in need of an originator. As for the second premise—which is the major of the syllogism—its evidence is that if He were not necessarily existent, He would be contingently existent (ja'iz al-wujud), and if He were contingently existent, He would need an originator to originate Him, and then that originator would be likewise. If the situation continues thus ad infinitum, infinite regress (tasalsul) occurs, and if the matter goes back to the first, circularity (dawr) occurs. Both circularity and infinite regress are impossible. So that which leads to them—namely His need for an originator—is impossible, and that which leads to it—namely His being contingently existent—is impossible. Hence its opposite is established, namely His being necessarily existent.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage is a logical proof (syllogism) for God's necessary existence. The author presents a syllogism with two premises. The minor premise states that the entire world depends on God; its evidence is that the world is originated and thus needs an originator (God). The major premise states that whoever the world depends on must be necessarily existent; its evidence is that if He were contingent, He would need another originator, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul) or circularity (dawr), both of which are impossible. Therefore, the only possibility is that He is necessarily existent. The argument follows a standard kalam cosmological argument structure.

Misconception: Some think the proof assumes God's existence without evidence.
Correction: The proof does not assume God's existence; it starts from the observable fact that the world is originated (contingent) and argues that this requires a necessary originator to avoid infinite regress or circularity.
