Maturidi View on Happiness and Misery
Maturidi definition of happiness and misery and their reversal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi definition of happiness (sa'ada) and misery (shaqawa) and how they can reverse at the moment of death.

Core Idea
For the Maturidis, happiness is present faith, misery is present disbelief—and both can flip at death.

The Maturidi school offers a distinctive perspective on what constitutes true happiness and misery. Rather than focusing solely on the final outcome, they define these states based on one's current faith or disbelief. This passage presents their view and its dramatic consequence: a disbeliever who dies in faith turns misery into happiness, and a believer who dies in disbelief suffers the opposite reversal.

Guided Path
1
Read the Maturidi definition
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully, noting the key terms sa'ada and shaqawa.
The Maturidis define happiness as faith in the present, not just in the afterlife.
2
Observe the reversal principle
Notice how the passage describes the reversal of states at death. This is a crucial point that distinguishes the Maturidis from Ash'aris.
3
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: what does this definition imply about the value of present faith? How might this affect one's view of final destiny?
This connects to the next lesson on saying 'I am a believer if Allah wills.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهب الماتريدية إلى أن السعادة هي الإيمان في الحال، والشقاوة هي الكفر في الحال، وإذا مات الكافر على الإيمان فقد انقلب شقاؤه سعادة، وإذا مات المؤمن على الكفر فقد انقلبت سعادته شقاوة. ويترتب على هذا الخلاف في أنه هل يجوز أن يقول الإنسان: أنا مؤمن إن شاء الله، أو لا يجوز ذلك؟ فعلى مذهب الأشاعرة يجوز أن يقول ذلك; لأن المعني بهذه العبارة عندهم: أنا أرجو أن يشاء الله لي أن أموت على الإيمان، وعند الماتريدية لا يجوز للإنسان أن يقول ذلك; لأن حاصل هذه العبارة على مذهبهم موهم أنه شاك في إيمانه في الحال، أو أنه يعلق حصول إيمانه الآن على المشيئة، وكلاهما قادح في حصول الإيمان. ومن العلماء من جعل الخلاف في هذه المسألة بين أئمة الشريعة; فقال: جوز الإمام الشافعي أن يقول الإنسان: أنا مؤمن إن شاء الله، ومنعه مالك وأبو حنيفة، وقال بعض أتباع الإمام مالك: إنه يجب على الإنسان أن يقول ذلك. ولا شك أن الخلاف في جواز إطلاق هذه العبارة ومنعها خاص بحالة ما إذا لم يرد المتكلم بها أنه شاك في الإيمان، وما إذا لم يرد التبرك بذكر اسم الله تعالى; فأما إذا قال ذلك وهو يريد أنه شاك فهو غير جائز بالإجماع; لأن الشك في إيمان نفسه كفر; وإذا قال ذلك وهو لا يريد إلا التبرك بذكر اسم الله تعالى فإنه جائز بالإجماع.
And the Maturidis hold that happiness is faith in the present state, and misery is disbelief in the present state, and if the disbeliever dies upon faith then his misery has turned into happiness, and if the believer dies upon disbelief – God forbid – then his happiness has turned into misery. And upon this difference depends the question of whether it is permissible for a person to say: 'I am a believer if Allah wills' or not? For according to the Ash'aris, it is permissible to say that; because the meaning of this expression for them is: 'I hope that Allah wills for me to die upon faith.' And according to the Maturidis, it is not permissible for a person to say that; because the gist of this expression in their view suggests that he is doubtful about his faith in the present, or that he conditions the attainment of his faith now upon the will, and both are detrimental to the attainment of faith. And among the scholars are those who placed the disagreement on this issue among the imams of the Shari'a; he said: Imam al-Shafi'i permitted a person to say 'I am a believer if Allah wills,' while Malik and Abu Hanifa forbade it, and some followers of Imam Malik said: it is obligatory for a person to say that. And there is no doubt that the disagreement on the permissibility or prohibition of uttering this expression is specific to the case when the speaker does not intend by it that he is doubtful about faith, and when he does not intend blessing by mentioning the name of Allah Almighty. But if he says it intending that he is doubtful, it is impermissible by consensus, because doubt in one's own faith is disbelief. And if he says it intending only blessing by mentioning the name of Allah Almighty, then it is permissible by consensus.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage presents the Maturidi view on the nature of true happiness (sa'ada) and misery (shaqawa), contrasting it with the Ash'ari view. The Maturidis hold that sa'ada is faith in the present life and shaqawa is disbelief in the present life, and these states can change at death: a disbeliever who dies in faith becomes happy, and a believer who dies in disbelief becomes miserable. This directly leads to a practical ruling on whether one may say 'I am a believer if Allah wills.' The Ash'aris permit it because they interpret it as a hope for a good end. The Maturidis forbid it because it implies doubt or conditioning of present faith. The passage then cites a dispute among the imams: al-Shafi'i permitted the expression, while Malik, Abu Hanifa, and some Malikis required or forbade it. Finally, it clarifies the consensus: the disagreement applies only when the speaker does not intend doubt or mere blessing. If doubt is intended, it is disbelief; if only blessing, it is unanimously permissible.

Key Terms
السعادة (al-sa'ada)
الشقاوة (al-shaqawa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Believing happiness and misery are fixed in this life and cannot change at death.
Correction: The Maturidis teach that these states can reverse based on one's final belief at death.

Checkpoint
According to the Maturidis, what happens to a disbeliever who dies upon faith?
A) He remains in misery.
B) His misery turns into happiness.
C) He becomes neither happy nor miserable.
D) He remains a disbeliever.

Key Takeaway
For the Maturidis, sa'ada is present faith, but its ultimate realization depends on dying upon that faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as if your current faith guarantees your final state, or are you actively securing your end by clinging to faith until death?
?
What would it take for you to truly fear—not just acknowledge—the possibility of dying in disbelief, and how does that fear shape your daily choices?
?
If your happiness and misery hinge on your final breath, are you investing more in your present comfort or in the state you will meet Allah with?

The Ruling on Saying 'I am a believer if Allah wills'
Disagreement on saying 'I am a believer if Allah wills' and its resolution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the disagreement between Ash'aris and Maturidis on saying 'I am a believer if Allah wills', as well as the positions of the early imams and the consensus on its intention.

Core Idea
The expression 'I am a believer if Allah wills' is interpreted differently by Ash'aris and Maturidis, leading to opposing rulings.

This lesson delves into a practical consequence of the differing definitions of happiness and misery: whether one can say 'I am a believer if Allah wills.' The Ash'aris permit it as an expression of hope for a good end, while the Maturidis forbid it as implying doubt. The passage also records the views of imams al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Abu Hanifa, and concludes with a consensus-based qualification that clarifies when the expression is prohibited or permissible.

Guided Path
1
Understand the link to the previous lesson
Recall that the Maturidis define happiness as present faith and misery as present disbelief. This shapes their view on the expression.
The Ash'aris have a different definition that we don't have here, but the passage focuses on the practical dispute.
2
Read the positions of the two schools
Find the Ash'ari interpretation (hope for a good end) and the Maturidi interpretation (implies doubt or conditioning).
3
Observe the additional scholarly disagreement
Note the views of al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Abu Hanifa. The passage mentions a someone who placed the disagreement among these imams.
Some Malikis even made it obligatory.
4
Study the final consensus qualification
The passage clarifies that the disagreement is only when the speaker does not intend doubt or mere blessing. If doubt is intended, it's disbelief; if only blessing, it's permissible by consensus.
This resolves the apparent contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهب الماتريدية إلى أن السعادة هي الإيمان في الحال، والشقاوة هي الكفر في الحال، وإذا مات الكافر على الإيمان فقد انقلب شقاؤه سعادة، وإذا مات المؤمن على الكفر فقد انقلبت سعادته شقاوة. ويترتب على هذا الخلاف في أنه هل يجوز أن يقول الإنسان: أنا مؤمن إن شاء الله، أو لا يجوز ذلك؟ فعلى مذهب الأشاعرة يجوز أن يقول ذلك; لأن المعني بهذه العبارة عندهم: أنا أرجو أن يشاء الله لي أن أموت على الإيمان، وعند الماتريدية لا يجوز للإنسان أن يقول ذلك; لأن حاصل هذه العبارة على مذهبهم موهم أنه شاك في إيمانه في الحال، أو أنه يعلق حصول إيمانه الآن على المشيئة، وكلاهما قادح في حصول الإيمان. ومن العلماء من جعل الخلاف في هذه المسألة بين أئمة الشريعة; فقال: جوز الإمام الشافعي أن يقول الإنسان: أنا مؤمن إن شاء الله، ومنعه مالك وأبو حنيفة، وقال بعض أتباع الإمام مالك: إنه يجب على الإنسان أن يقول ذلك. ولا شك أن الخلاف في جواز إطلاق هذه العبارة ومنعها خاص بحالة ما إذا لم يرد المتكلم بها أنه شاك في الإيمان، وما إذا لم يرد التبرك بذكر اسم الله تعالى; فأما إذا قال ذلك وهو يريد أنه شاك فهو غير جائز بالإجماع; لأن الشك في إيمان نفسه كفر; وإذا قال ذلك وهو لا يريد إلا التبرك بذكر اسم الله تعالى فإنه جائز بالإجماع.
And the Maturidis hold that happiness is faith in the present state, and misery is disbelief in the present state, and if the disbeliever dies upon faith then his misery has turned into happiness, and if the believer dies upon disbelief – God forbid – then his happiness has turned into misery. And upon this difference depends the question of whether it is permissible for a person to say: 'I am a believer if Allah wills' or not? For according to the Ash'aris, it is permissible to say that; because the meaning of this expression for them is: 'I hope that Allah wills for me to die upon faith.' And according to the Maturidis, it is not permissible for a person to say that; because the gist of this expression in their view suggests that he is doubtful about his faith in the present, or that he conditions the attainment of his faith now upon the will, and both are detrimental to the attainment of faith. And among the scholars are those who placed the disagreement on this issue among the imams of the Shari'a; he said: Imam al-Shafi'i permitted a person to say 'I am a believer if Allah wills,' while Malik and Abu Hanifa forbade it, and some followers of Imam Malik said: it is obligatory for a person to say that. And there is no doubt that the disagreement on the permissibility or prohibition of uttering this expression is specific to the case when the speaker does not intend by it that he is doubtful about faith, and when he does not intend blessing by mentioning the name of Allah Almighty. But if he says it intending that he is doubtful, it is impermissible by consensus, because doubt in one's own faith is disbelief. And if he says it intending only blessing by mentioning the name of Allah Almighty, then it is permissible by consensus.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage presents the Maturidi view on the nature of true happiness (sa'ada) and misery (shaqawa), contrasting it with the Ash'ari view. The Maturidis hold that sa'ada is faith in the present life and shaqawa is disbelief in the present life, and these states can change at death: a disbeliever who dies in faith becomes happy, and a believer who dies in disbelief becomes miserable. This directly leads to a practical ruling on whether one may say 'I am a believer if Allah wills.' The Ash'aris permit it because they interpret it as a hope for a good end. The Maturidis forbid it because it implies doubt or conditioning of present faith. The passage then cites a dispute among the imams: al-Shafi'i permitted the expression, while Malik, Abu Hanifa, and some Malikis required or forbade it. Finally, it clarifies the consensus: the disagreement applies only when the speaker does not intend doubt or mere blessing. If doubt is intended, it is disbelief; if only blessing, it is unanimously permissible.

Key Terms
أنا مؤمن إن شاء الله (I am a believer if Allah wills)
الأشاعرة (Ash'aris)
الماتريدية (Maturidis)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking the dispute is about whether one can be uncertain about one's own faith.
Correction: The dispute is about the interpretation of the expression: does it imply doubt or hope? The consensus prohibits it if doubt is intended, but allows it if merely invoking blessing.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the consensus ruling if a person says 'I am a believer if Allah wills' intending only blessing?
A) Permissible
B) Forbidden
C) Recommended
D) Disliked

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of saying 'I am a believer if Allah wills' hinges on the speaker's intention: it is permitted as an expression of hope or blessing, but forbidden if it implies doubt.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'insha'Allah' about your own faith, do you truly mean it as a hope for the future or is there a hidden doubt about your present state? How can you be honest with yourself and ensure your intention is pure?
?
The passage states that doubting one's own faith is kufr. Have you ever felt doubts about your iman? How do you reconcile that with the necessity of certainty, and what steps can you take to strengthen your conviction?
?
The scholars differed on this expression. Does your personal practice align with any of these positions? Are you using 'insha'Allah' as a blessing or as an escape from certainty? What does your use of this phrase reveal about your heart's condition?

Quranic and Rational Proof that the Servant is Not the Creator
The author defines kasb and gives Quranic and rational proofs against human creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni position on human action (kasb) and the Quranic and rational arguments that the servant does not create his own acts.

Core Idea
Humans incline toward actions, but only Allah creates them.

The author begins by clarifying the Sunni doctrine of acquisition (kasb): the servant does not originate or create his actions but merely has a relation of preponderance. He then provides two clear Quranic verses as the scriptural foundation, followed by a logical syllogism that rationally demonstrates the impossibility of human creation. This lesson unpacks both proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the literal translation. Notice the author's concise style and the shift from scriptural to rational argument.
The passage contains two distinct proofs: Quranic and rational.
2
Understand the concept of kasb
The author states the servant is not an originator or creator (mūjid, khāliq) of actions, but has 'nisbat al-tarjīḥ' – a relation of preponderance or inclination. This is the core of the Sunni position.
Tarjīḥ means giving preference to one option over another.
3
Examine the Quranic verses
Identify the two verses: 'He created everything' (Al-Furqan 25:2) and 'Allah created you and what you do' (As-Saffat 37:96). Reflect on how they support the idea that Allah alone creates.
The second verse is often used in debates against those who claim humans create their actions.
4
Analyze the conditional syllogism
The author presents a logical argument: if the servant were a creator, he would know all details of his actions; but we observe ignorance of details; therefore, the servant is not a creator. Map out the antecedent, consequent, and the negation.
This is a qiyās shartī (conditional syllogism) – a standard kalam tool.
5
Connect to the broader theology
Consider how this proof preserves divine omnipotence while still allowing human responsibility (through acquisition). This is a key issue in Sunni theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة كسب الأفعال غير أنْ يكونَ مُوجدا وخالقا لها ، وإِنَّما له فيها نسبة الترجيح، كالميل للفعل أو التّرك، والأصل في هذا قوله تعالى: ﴿وَخَلَقَ كُلَّ شَيْءٍ فَقَدَّرَهُ تَقْدِيرًا﴾ [الفرقان: 2] . ﴿ وَاللهُ خَلَقَكُمْ وَمَا تَعْمَلُونَ ﴾ [الصافات: 96] . ولو كانَ العبد خالِقا لأفعاله لكانَ عالما بتفاصيلها ، واللَّازِم باطل ؛ فالملزوم كذلكَ .
Issue of the acquisition of actions: [The servant is] not such that he is an originator and creator of them, but rather he has in them a relation of preponderance, like inclination towards the action or its abandonment. The basis for this is His saying: 'And He created everything and determined it with due determination' (Al-Furqan 25:2). And 'And Allah created you and what you do' (As-Saffat 37:96). And if the servant were a creator of his actions, he would be knowledgeable of their details. But the consequent is false, so the antecedent is likewise false.
Source pages p. 348

Commentary
This passage presents both a Quranic and a rational proof. First, it restates the Sunni position: the servant does not create his actions (he is not mūjid or khāliq), but has only a relation of preponderance (nisbat al-tarjīḥ), i.e., an inclination towards act or omission. Then two Quranic verses are cited as the foundation: (1) 'He created everything and determined it precisely' – implying all things, including actions, are created by God; (2) 'Allah created you and what you do' – directly refuting the view that humans create their deeds. Next, a rational proof is given in the form of a conditional syllogism (qiyās shartī). The conditional premise: 'If the servant were a creator of his actions, he would be knowledgeable of their details.' The minor premise: 'But the consequent is false' – because we observe that people are ignorant of the countless minute movements and components of their own actions (e.g., muscle motions while walking). Therefore, the antecedent (that the servant creates his acts) is false. This syllogism logically establishes that human actions are not independently created by the servant.

Key Terms
ترجيح
قياس شرطي
ملزوم
لازم
خالق
مُوجد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Humans independently create their own actions.
Correction: This passage shows that creation belongs to Allah alone; humans only have inclination (tarjīḥ) and acquire responsibility, not independent creation.

Checkpoint
What is the consequent (lāzim) in the conditional syllogism, and why is it false?
A) 'The servant is a creator'; false because only God creates
B) 'The servant would know the details of his actions'; false because humans are ignorant of many details
C) 'The servant is free'; false because the Quran denies freedom
D) 'Actions exist'; false because they are illusions

Key Takeaway
The Quran and reason both prove that Allah alone creates all actions; the servant only has inclination and is not an independent creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform a good deed, do you feel a sense of pride as if you created that action? How would your heart change if you truly internalized that Allah is the sole creator of that act, and you only had an inclination? Will you practice saying 'La hawla wa la quwwata illa billah' whenever you accomplish something good?
?
Consider a sin you struggle with. Do you ever think you are the one bringing that sin into existence? This lesson teaches that even your sins are created by Allah, yet you are responsible for your inclination. Does this make you fear Allah more or less? Will you repent and ask Allah to change your inclination rather than relying on your own strength?
?
If you sincerely affirm that your actions are not your creation, then how does that affect your dua? Do you ask Allah to create good actions for you? Are you willing to surrender the illusion of control and beg Allah to make you inclined toward good? What concrete step will you take today to rely on Allah for your actions?

Allah's Reward and Punishment Are by Grace and Justice
Bajuri states the Ash'ari position and defines key terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position that Allah's reward and punishment are based on pure grace and pure justice, not on any obligation.

Core Idea
Allah rewards by grace and punishes by justice, not by compulsion.

This lesson introduces the foundational statement that Allah is not obliged to reward obedience or punish disobedience. The author defines pure grace as giving by choice, rejecting the views of philosophers (who claim necessitation) and Mu'tazila (who claim obligation).

Guided Path
1
Read the opening statement
Begin with the Arabic: 'بيان أنه تعالى لا يجب عليه ثواب المطيع ولا عقاب العصاة'. Notice the negation of obligation.
The phrase sets the Ash'ari position clearly.
2
Understand pure grace and justice
Learn that 'محض الفضل' means giving by complete choice, and 'محض العدل' means placing things in their proper place without objection.
This refutes both determinism and moral obligation.
3
Identify the two refuted groups
The passage states the purpose is to refute philosophers (who deny divine choice) and Mu'tazila (who claim reward is a duty).
Know their errors to appreciate the Ash'ari stance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان أنه تعالى لا يجب عليه ثواب المطيع ولا عقاب العصاة. أي: بفضله الخالص، وهو: العطاء عن اختيار، لا عن إيجاب كما يقوله الحكماء، ولا عن وجوب كما يقوله المعتزلة، وإن يعذب فبمحض العدل أي فتعذيبه بعدله الخالص، وهو: وضع الشيء في محله من غير اعتراض على الفاعل. من الكلام السابق المشار إليه، وتقدير الكلام على هذا الوجه: إذا علمت أن الله تعالى منفرد بخلق أفعال العباد الاختيارية خيرًا كانت أو شرًا فاعلم أنه إن يثبنا... إلخ. والمَحْضُ - بفتح الميم وسكون الحاء المهملة - وصف بمعنى الخالص من الشوائب، وإضافته إلى الفضل من إضافة الصفة إلى الموصوف، والمراد من محض الفضل العطاء الصادر منه تعالى عن اختيار كامل لا تشوبه شائبة إيجاب ولا شائبة وجوب، والغرض من هذا الكلام الرد على فرقتين: الفرقة الأولى الفلاسفة، والفرقة الثانية: المعتزلة. فأما الفلاسفة: فزعموا أن الله تعالى يثيب الطائعين بغير اختيار له في إثابتهم، لكون طاعتهم علة تنشأ عنها معلولاتها من غير اختيار لها. وأما المعتزلة فزعموا أن إثابة الطائعين مستحقة عليه سبحانه، يثيبهم باختياره، لكن لكونه يقبح ألا يفعل ذلك معهم. ودليل ما ذهب إليه أهل السنة: أن طاعات العباد وإن كثرت لا تفي ببعض ما أنعم الله به على العبد، فكيف يتصور يستحق العوض عليها؟ لأن قسم الإلهيات اعتراض على الفاعل، وليس ظلما ولا جورًا، ولا واجبًا عليه تعالى أن يفعله؛ جميع الكائنات التي من جملتها الثواب والعقاب مملوك له تعالى، ناشئ عن قدرته وإرادته؛ فليس لهما سبب عقلي، وإنما الطاعة والمعصية أمارتان مخلوقتان له تعالى تدلان على ما اختاره من ثواب وعقاب، حتى لو عكس دلالتهما، أو أثاب وعاقب بلا سبق أمارة؛ لكان ذلك منه تعالى حسنا، لا يسأل عما يفعل، إلا أن الخلف في الوعد نقص لا يجوز أن ينسب إليه تعالى، فيثيب المطيع البتة إنجازا لوعده، بخلاف الخلف في الوعيد، فإنه فضل وكرم، يجوز إسناده إليه تعالى، فيجوز أن لا يعاقب العاصي.
An explanation that He, exalted, is not obligated to reward the obedient nor punish the disobedient. That is: by His pure grace, which is giving by choice, not by necessitation as the philosophers say, nor by obligation as the Mu'tazila say. And 'if He punishes, it is by pure justice', meaning His punishment is by His pure justice, which is placing a thing in its proper place without objection against the agent. From the previous discourse referred to, and the estimation of the statement in this manner: when you know that Allah, exalted, is unique in creating the voluntary acts of servants, whether good or evil, then know that if He rewards us... etc. And 'al-mahḍ' is a description meaning pure from impurities, and its addition to 'grace' is addition of the adjective to the described. The intended meaning of 'pure grace' is the giving emanating from Him, exalted, by complete choice, untainted by any trace of necessitation or obligation. The purpose of this speech is to refute two groups: the first group, the philosophers, and the second group, the Mu'tazila. As for the philosophers, they claimed that Allah, exalted, rewards the obedient without choice on His part in rewarding them, because their obedience is a cause that generates its effects without choice. As for the Mu'tazila, they claimed that rewarding the obedient is due upon Him, exalted; He rewards them by His choice, but because it is ugly for Him not to do so with them. And the proof of what Ahl al-Sunna uphold: that the acts of obedience of servants, even if numerous, do not suffice for some of what Allah has bestowed upon the servant, so how can it be imagined that he deserves compensation for them? Because the section on divine matters is an objection against the agent, and it is neither oppression nor injustice, nor obligatory upon Him, exalted, to do it; all beings, including reward and punishment, are owned by Him, exalted, arising from His power and will; so there is no rational cause for them. Rather, obedience and disobedience are two customary indicators created by Him, exalted, indicating what He has chosen of reward and punishment, such that even if He reversed their indication, or rewarded and punished without prior indication, that would be good from Him, exalted; He is not asked about what He does. However, breaking a promise is a deficiency that cannot be attributed to Him, exalted; so He rewards the obedient definitely in fulfillment of His promise, unlike breaking a threat, for it is grace and generosity, and it is permissible to attribute it to Him, exalted; so it is permissible that He does not punish the disobedient.
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage is a theological explanation and refutation. It begins by stating the Ash'ari position that Allah is not obligated to reward the obedient or punish the disobedient. Reward is by pure grace (faḍl) and punishment by pure justice (ʿadl). Grace implies free choice, not the necessitation (ījāb) of the philosophers who see divine action as a natural emanation, nor the obligation (wujūb) of the Mu'tazila who think reward is a required act. The author then clarifies that the phrase 'pure grace' means giving by complete choice, free from any taint of necessitation. He states the purpose: to rebut two groups. The philosophers hold that God's reward is involuntary, like a cause producing its effect. The Mu'tazila hold that reward is a duty on God, because it would be morally ugly (qabīḥ) for Him to withhold it. The author then gives the Ash'ari proof: human acts of obedience cannot repay even a fraction of God's blessings, so how could they deserve reward? All events, including reward and punishment, are God's property, subject to His power and will; there is no rational cause necessitating them. Obedience and disobedience are merely customary indicators (amārāt) created by God to signify His chosen outcome. Even if God reversed the indicators or rewarded/punished without them, it would be good, for He is not questioned about His actions. However, breaking a promise is a deficiency (naqṣ) that cannot be attributed to God, so He must reward the obedient to fulfill His promise. In contrast, breaking a threat is grace and generosity, so He may forgive the disobedient. This passage thus defends divine sovereignty and freedom against rationalist constraints.

Key Terms
فضل
عدل
إيجاب
وجوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ash'aris believe Allah can arbitrarily break His promise.
Correction: This passage only establishes the basis of grace; it does not address promise-keeping, which is covered later.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why do the Mu'tazila think Allah rewards the obedient?
A) Because obedience causes reward necessarily.
B) Because it is a moral duty and ugly to withhold.
C) Because Allah has no choice.
D) Because reward is a natural property of obedience.

Key Takeaway
Allah's actions of reward and punishment are based on His pure grace and justice, free from any external necessitation or moral obligation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever feel that your good deeds entitle you to Allah's reward? How does this lesson challenge that feeling? What would change if you truly believed every blessing is pure grace, not a wage?
?
When you face a trial or punishment for a sin, do you accept it as pure justice from Allah, or do you feel He has been unfair? How can this passage reshape your response to hardship?
?
Do you treat your obedience to Allah as a transaction, expecting something in return? How can you shift to a mindset of gratitude for His unmerited favor, even for the ability to obey?

The Ash'ari Proof: Why Reward Cannot Be Obligatory
Bajuri provides the Ash'ari proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the evidence from Ahl al-Sunna that reward is not due because acts of obedience are insufficient and all things belong to Allah.

Core Idea
No act of obedience can earn reward; all is by Allah's choice.

This lesson presents the proof that Ahl al-Sunna use to show reward is not obligatory. The argument states that even numerous acts of obedience cannot repay a fraction of Allah's blessings, so how could they deserve reward? All events, including reward and punishment, are owned by Allah and arise from His power and will.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof in Arabic
Start with 'ودليل ما ذهب إليه أهل السنة: أن طاعات العباد...' and follow the logic.
The proof is a rational argument.
2
Understand the insufficiency of acts
The passage says acts of obedience do not repay even some of Allah's blessings. Reflect on why this negates deserving reward.
This is a key point against the Mu'tazila.
3
Learn about divine ownership
All beings are owned by Allah, so reward and punishment are His property. There is no rational cause; they are merely indicative signs.
This refutes the philosophers' causality.
4
Consider the reversal possibility
Even if Allah reversed the indicators or acted without them, it would be good. This underscores divine freedom.
This reinforces that there's no obligation.

بيان أنه تعالى لا يجب عليه ثواب المطيع ولا عقاب العصاة. أي: بفضله الخالص، وهو: العطاء عن اختيار، لا عن إيجاب كما يقوله الحكماء، ولا عن وجوب كما يقوله المعتزلة، وإن يعذب فبمحض العدل أي فتعذيبه بعدله الخالص، وهو: وضع الشيء في محله من غير اعتراض على الفاعل. من الكلام السابق المشار إليه، وتقدير الكلام على هذا الوجه: إذا علمت أن الله تعالى منفرد بخلق أفعال العباد الاختيارية خيرًا كانت أو شرًا فاعلم أنه إن يثبنا... إلخ. والمَحْضُ - بفتح الميم وسكون الحاء المهملة - وصف بمعنى الخالص من الشوائب، وإضافته إلى الفضل من إضافة الصفة إلى الموصوف، والمراد من محض الفضل العطاء الصادر منه تعالى عن اختيار كامل لا تشوبه شائبة إيجاب ولا شائبة وجوب، والغرض من هذا الكلام الرد على فرقتين: الفرقة الأولى الفلاسفة، والفرقة الثانية: المعتزلة. فأما الفلاسفة: فزعموا أن الله تعالى يثيب الطائعين بغير اختيار له في إثابتهم، لكون طاعتهم علة تنشأ عنها معلولاتها من غير اختيار لها. وأما المعتزلة فزعموا أن إثابة الطائعين مستحقة عليه سبحانه، يثيبهم باختياره، لكن لكونه يقبح ألا يفعل ذلك معهم. ودليل ما ذهب إليه أهل السنة: أن طاعات العباد وإن كثرت لا تفي ببعض ما أنعم الله به على العبد، فكيف يتصور يستحق العوض عليها؟ لأن قسم الإلهيات اعتراض على الفاعل، وليس ظلما ولا جورًا، ولا واجبًا عليه تعالى أن يفعله؛ جميع الكائنات التي من جملتها الثواب والعقاب مملوك له تعالى، ناشئ عن قدرته وإرادته؛ فليس لهما سبب عقلي، وإنما الطاعة والمعصية أمارتان مخلوقتان له تعالى تدلان على ما اختاره من ثواب وعقاب، حتى لو عكس دلالتهما، أو أثاب وعاقب بلا سبق أمارة؛ لكان ذلك منه تعالى حسنا، لا يسأل عما يفعل، إلا أن الخلف في الوعد نقص لا يجوز أن ينسب إليه تعالى، فيثيب المطيع البتة إنجازا لوعده، بخلاف الخلف في الوعيد، فإنه فضل وكرم، يجوز إسناده إليه تعالى، فيجوز أن لا يعاقب العاصي.
An explanation that He, exalted, is not obligated to reward the obedient nor punish the disobedient. That is: by His pure grace, which is giving by choice, not by necessitation as the philosophers say, nor by obligation as the Mu'tazila say. And 'if He punishes, it is by pure justice', meaning His punishment is by His pure justice, which is placing a thing in its proper place without objection against the agent. From the previous discourse referred to, and the estimation of the statement in this manner: when you know that Allah, exalted, is unique in creating the voluntary acts of servants, whether good or evil, then know that if He rewards us... etc. And 'al-mahḍ' is a description meaning pure from impurities, and its addition to 'grace' is addition of the adjective to the described. The intended meaning of 'pure grace' is the giving emanating from Him, exalted, by complete choice, untainted by any trace of necessitation or obligation. The purpose of this speech is to refute two groups: the first group, the philosophers, and the second group, the Mu'tazila. As for the philosophers, they claimed that Allah, exalted, rewards the obedient without choice on His part in rewarding them, because their obedience is a cause that generates its effects without choice. As for the Mu'tazila, they claimed that rewarding the obedient is due upon Him, exalted; He rewards them by His choice, but because it is ugly for Him not to do so with them. And the proof of what Ahl al-Sunna uphold: that the acts of obedience of servants, even if numerous, do not suffice for some of what Allah has bestowed upon the servant, so how can it be imagined that he deserves compensation for them? Because the section on divine matters is an objection against the agent, and it is neither oppression nor injustice, nor obligatory upon Him, exalted, to do it; all beings, including reward and punishment, are owned by Him, exalted, arising from His power and will; so there is no rational cause for them. Rather, obedience and disobedience are two customary indicators created by Him, exalted, indicating what He has chosen of reward and punishment, such that even if He reversed their indication, or rewarded and punished without prior indication, that would be good from Him, exalted; He is not asked about what He does. However, breaking a promise is a deficiency that cannot be attributed to Him, exalted; so He rewards the obedient definitely in fulfillment of His promise, unlike breaking a threat, for it is grace and generosity, and it is permissible to attribute it to Him, exalted; so it is permissible that He does not punish the disobedient.
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage is a theological explanation and refutation. It begins by stating the Ash'ari position that Allah is not obligated to reward the obedient or punish the disobedient. Reward is by pure grace (faḍl) and punishment by pure justice (ʿadl). Grace implies free choice, not the necessitation (ījāb) of the philosophers who see divine action as a natural emanation, nor the obligation (wujūb) of the Mu'tazila who think reward is a required act. The author then clarifies that the phrase 'pure grace' means giving by complete choice, free from any taint of necessitation. He states the purpose: to rebut two groups. The philosophers hold that God's reward is involuntary, like a cause producing its effect. The Mu'tazila hold that reward is a duty on God, because it would be morally ugly (qabīḥ) for Him to withhold it. The author then gives the Ash'ari proof: human acts of obedience cannot repay even a fraction of God's blessings, so how could they deserve reward? All events, including reward and punishment, are God's property, subject to His power and will; there is no rational cause necessitating them. Obedience and disobedience are merely customary indicators (amārāt) created by God to signify His chosen outcome. Even if God reversed the indicators or rewarded/punished without them, it would be good, for He is not questioned about His actions. However, breaking a promise is a deficiency (naqṣ) that cannot be attributed to God, so He must reward the obedient to fulfill His promise. In contrast, breaking a threat is grace and generosity, so He may forgive the disobedient. This passage thus defends divine sovereignty and freedom against rationalist constraints.

Key Terms
أمارة
سبب عقلي
مملوك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ash'aris believe Allah could punish without cause arbitrarily.
Correction: The passage says even if Allah reversed indicators, it would be good; it does not say He does so arbitrarily. He acts with wisdom.

Checkpoint
According to the proof, why cannot acts of obedience earn reward?
A) Because they are few in number.
B) Because they do not repay Allah's blessings.
C) Because Allah is not just.
D) Because acts are not owned by Allah.

Key Takeaway
Reward is not obligatory because acts cannot repay Allah's blessings, and all things are under His ownership and will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How do you reconcile the idea that your acts of obedience cannot earn you any reward from Allah? Does this make you feel your efforts are meaningless, or does it increase your reliance on His mercy? What would change in your daily worship if you truly believed that your prayers and charity are not enough to repay even a single blessing from Allah?
?
The passage states that even if Allah reversed the indicators of obedience and disobedience, it would be good. How does this affect your understanding of justice? If Allah chose to punish the obedient and reward the disobedient, would you still submit to His will? What does your answer reveal about your true belief in His absolute sovereignty?
?
You often ask Allah for reward and fear punishment. But this lesson teaches that reward and punishment are not owed to you. How does this knowledge change the way you approach your Lord — do you still seek reward for your deeds, or do you seek Him alone? What would it look like to worship without expectation of return?

Promise vs Threat: Why Allah Must Reward but May Forgive
Bajuri distinguishes promise vs threat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between breaking a promise (which is a deficiency) and breaking a threat (which is grace), and how this relates to Allah's actions.

Core Idea
Allah must keep His promise but may forgive threats.

This lesson clarifies a crucial nuance. Although Allah is not obligated to reward, He does so because breaking a promise would be a deficiency (naqṣ) unworthy of Him. But breaking a threat (i.e., forgiving sinners) is a manifestation of grace and generosity, so it is permissible.

Guided Path
1
Read the final part of the passage
Focus on 'إلا أن الخلف في الوعد نقص...' and note the contrast between promise and threat.
This is a key Ash'ari teaching.
2
Understand why promise must be kept
Breaking a promise is a deficiency, so Allah will definitely reward the obedient to fulfill His promise.
Think about why this does not contradict the earlier statement about no obligation.
3
Understand why threat may be broken
Breaking a threat is grace and generosity, so Allah may forgive the disobedient.
This shows Allah's mercy.

Refuting the Mu'tazilite Obligation of Benefit
Bajuri refutes the Mu'tazilite claim of obligatory benefit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash'ari school rejects the Mu'tazilite claim that God is obligated to do what is beneficial, and why breaking a threat is permissible while breaking a promise is not.

Core Idea
Nothing is obligatory upon God; His actions are free, but His promise is binding.

The Mu'tazilites argued that God must always do what is most beneficial for His creatures. In this passage, Bajuri sharply rejects that claim, stating that nothing is obligatory upon God. He then clarifies the difference between divine promises (which cannot be broken) and divine threats (which may be broken out of grace). This distinction preserves God's absolute freedom while upholding His truthfulness.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the strong opening: 'their statement is false; nothing is obligatory upon Him.'
2
Distinguish rational vs. legal considerations
Bajuri first affirms what reason requires (no obligation on God), then explains the legal perspective (promises and threats). Compare the two levels.
3
Understand promise vs. threat
A promise of reward cannot be broken because it would imply deficiency. But a threat of punishment may be broken, because withholding punishment is an act of grace and generosity.
This is a key Ash'ari doctrine: God's mercy can override His justice.
4
Reflect on the supplication
The passage ends with a prayer asking God to treat us with gentleness and favor. Consider how this reflects the author's theological humility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نقض قاعدة الصلاح والأصلح الاعتزالية ] وَقَولُهُمْ إِنَّ الصَّلاحَ واجِبُ عليه زُورٌ مَا عَلَيْهِ وَاجِبُ وأشار إلى المسألة المُترجمة في كتبهم بمسألة وجوب الصلاح فاعلها، وهذا كله تقرير لما يقتضيه العقل، وأما بحسب الشرع فإن الله سبحانه وعد الطائع بالثواب، ووعد العاصي بالعقاب، ولا يجوز خلف الوعد على ما قررناه آنفا، ويجوز خلف الوعيد؛ لأن خلف الوعيد فضل وكرم، وهو سبحانه أكرم المسئولين، وأوسعهم رحابًا، وأكثرهم منًّا، عاملنا الله تعالى بلطفه، وفضله، ومنه! آمين.
Refutation of the Mu'tazilite principle of al-ṣalāḥ wa al-aṣlaḥ. And their statement that al-ṣalāḥ is obligatory upon Him is false; nothing is obligatory upon Him. And he referred to the issue translated in their books as the issue of the obligation of al-ṣalāḥ. Its agent? And all this is a confirmation of what reason requires. As for according to the law, Allah, glorified, promised the obedient reward, and promised the disobedient punishment, and breaking the promise is not permissible as we established earlier, while breaking the threat is permissible, because breaking the threat is grace and generosity. And He, glorified, is the most generous of those asked, and the most vast in mercy, and the most abundant in favor. May Allah treat us with His gentleness, grace, and favor! Amen.
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite doctrine of al-ṣalāḥ wa al-aṣlaḥ (the beneficial and the most beneficial). The author states that the Mu'tazilite claim that doing what is beneficial (ṣalāḥ) is obligatory upon God is false; nothing is obligatory upon God. He notes that this issue is known in Mu'tazilite books as the obligation of al-ṣalāḥ. Then he distinguishes between rational and legal considerations: reason does not impose any obligation on God, but according to revelation, God promised reward to the obedient and punishment to the disobedient. Breaking a promise is impermissible because it would imply deficiency, as earlier explained. However, breaking a threat is permissible because it is an act of grace and generosity. The passage ends with a supplication, humbly asking God to treat us with His gentleness and favor. This concisely upholds divine freedom against rationalist moral requirements.

Key Terms
صلاح
أصلح
وجوب
خلف الوعيد
فضل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that Ash'aris believe God can arbitrarily break His promises.
Correction: The passage clearly states that breaking a promise is not permissible, while breaking a threat is permissible as an act of grace.

Checkpoint
What is the Ash'ari response to the Mu'tazilite claim that doing what is beneficial is obligatory upon God?
A) It is true; God must do what is most beneficial.
B) It is false; nothing is obligatory upon God.
C) It is true only if God promises it.
D) It is false but God voluntarily chooses the beneficial.

Key Takeaway
God is not obligated to do what is beneficial; His promise is binding, but His threat can be set aside by grace.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Has there been a time when you felt God 'owed' you something because of your worship? How does this passage's assertion that 'nothing is obligatory upon Him' challenge your sense of entitlement before God? What would it look like to truly affirm that every blessing is a gift, not a wage?
?
You believe God promised reward for your obedience, but do you also live in fear that His threat of punishment will certainly fall on you? How does knowing that God may break His threat out of grace change the way you view your failures? Will you now turn to Him with hope rather than despair?
?
If God is not obligated to do anything for you, then why do you think He has created you, guided you, and promised you Paradise? When you reflect on His generosity despite His freedom, does your heart overflow with gratitude? If not, what is holding you back from a deeper love of the One who chose to be merciful?

Refutation of the Mu'tazilite Analogy from God's Independence
Quick Sunni response to the Mu'tazilite analogy of command implying will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the command to act kindly does not imply that God must do al-aslah, because He is absolutely independent.

Core Idea
God's absolute independence breaks the Mu'tazilite analogy.

The Mu'tazilites argued that a command to do something kind implies the commander wills it. The Sunni response cuts this analogy by showing it only applies to dependent beings. God is not dependent on anyone, so the analogy fails. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get the flow.
Notice the contrast structure: human ruler vs. God.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite claim
The Mu'tazilite claim is that a command implies a will, so God's command to do good implies He wills it for His benefit.
3
Understand the Sunni counter
The Sunni response points out that such reasoning only holds for a dependent ruler. God is absolutely independent, so the analogy fails.
Key attributes: not in need, cannot be benefited or harmed.
4
Reflect on the attributes of God
List the divine attributes mentioned: independence, power, no need for supporters, etc. How do they dismantle the analogy?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلنا: أنَّ الأمر يستلزم الإرادة — إنما يكون ذلك من شأن حكيم محتاج إلى طاعةِ الأولياء أو رجوع الأعداء، يتعزّز بكثرة الأعوان والأنصار، وتعظم لديه الأقدار، لا في حكيم غني كلَّ الغِنَى عن مُوالاةِ الأولياء، قادر كلَّ القدرة على الانتقام من الأعداء، لا تنفعه طاعة من أطاعه، ولا تضره معاداة من عاداه، لا يعتز بكثرة الأنصار والأعوان، ولا تخذله الأعداء ولو كان بعدد القطر والحصى والتراب.

وقد استدلَّ أهل السنة والجماعة لصحة ما ذهبوا إليه من أنَّه لا يجب على الله تعالى فعل الصَّلاح والأصلح لأحدٍ من عباده بوجوه: الوجه الأول: أنه لو وجب عليه الأصلح لعباده لما خلق الكافِرَ الفقير المُعذَّبَ في الدُّنيا والآخرة، سيّما المبتلى بالأسقام والآلام والمِحَنِ والآفات. وقد حُكِي أن الحافظ ابن حجر مرَّ يوماً بالسُّوق في موكب عظيم وهَيَّاةٍ جَمِيلةٍ، فهجَمَ عليه يهودي وأثوابه مُلَطَّخةٌ بالدَّرَنِ، وهو في غاية الرَّثَاثة والبشاعة، فقَبَضَ على لِجَامِ بغلته، ثم قال له: يا شيخ الإسلام، أنت تزعم أن نبيكم قال: «الدنيا سجن المؤمن، وجنة الكافر»، فأي سجن هذا أنتَ فيه مع النعمة؟ وأي جنَّة أنا فيها مع ما ترى؟ فقال له الحافظ: أما أنا، فإنَّ الذي أنا فيه بالنسبة لما أعده الله في الآخرة مِن النّعيم للمؤمنين يُعدُّ سجناً، وأما أنت، فإن الذي أنت فيه بالنسبة لما ينتظرك من العذاب الأليم يُعَدُّ جَنَّةٌ.
We said: The command entails the will — that is only the concern of a wise person who is in need of the obedience of friends or the return of enemies, who gains strength through many supporters and helpers, and for whom positions become great. Not so for a Wise Being who is absolutely independent of the friendship of friends, absolutely able to take vengeance on enemies; the obedience of those who obey Him does not benefit Him, nor does the enmity of those who oppose Him harm Him; He is not strengthened by many helpers and supporters, nor do enemies weaken Him even if they were as numerous as the drops of rain, pebbles, and dust.

The People of the Sunnah and the Community have adduced proofs for the correctness of their position that it is not obligatory upon God Almighty to do what is proper or most proper for any of His servants in several ways: The first proof: that if the most proper were obligatory upon Him for His servants, He would not have created the poor disbeliever who is tormented in this world and the Hereafter, especially one afflicted with sicknesses, pains, trials, and afflictions. It has been related that al-Hafiz Ibn Hajar one day passed through the market in a great procession and a fine appearance. A Jew rushed upon him, his garments stained with filth, in the utmost shabbiness and ugliness. He grabbed the bridle of his mule and said to him: 'O Sheikh of Islam, you claim that your Prophet said: "The world is a prison for the believer and a paradise for the disbeliever." So what kind of prison is this that you are in, with blessing? And what kind of paradise am I in, with what you see?' Al-Hafiz said to him: 'As for me, what I am in, compared to what God has prepared in the Hereafter of bliss for the believers, is considered a prison. As for you, what you are in, compared to what awaits you of painful punishment, is considered a paradise.'
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage begins the Sunni refutation. First, there is a quick response to the Mu'tazilite analogy: the command to act kindly applies only to a wise person who is dependent on others; God is absolutely independent, so the analogy fails. Then the author introduces three proofs. The first proof is presented: if al-aslah were obligatory, God would not create a poor disbeliever who suffers in both worlds. The story of al-Hafiz Ibn Hajar illustrates this: a Jew in misery challenges the hadith that the world is a prison for the believer and a paradise for the disbeliever. Ibn Hajar replies that relative to the hereafter, the believer's worldly blessings are still a prison compared to paradise, and the disbeliever's worldly misery is a paradise compared to hellfire. Thus, the existence of suffering disbelievers shows that God is not obligated to give everyone the most advantageous situation; His wisdom and justice are not bound by human notions of al-aslah.

Key Terms
الاستلزام
الغني المطلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God commands kindness, He must will it for His own benefit.
Correction: God's commands are for our benefit, not His; He is independent of all need.

Checkpoint
What is the key difference between a human ruler and God in the argument?
A) Human ruler is independent
B) God is independent
C) Both are dependent
D) Neither is independent

Key Takeaway
God's absolute independence refutes the analogy that His command implies He must do what is best for His own sake.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you obey God in your daily life, do you unconsciously expect something in return—a blessing, protection, or reward? How does the truth that God gains nothing from your obedience challenge you to worship for His sake alone, not for what you can get? What practical step can you take today to purify your intention in one act of worship?
?
Think of a time you felt that your sin or failure somehow 'let God down' or 'harmed' your relationship with Him. How does this lesson's emphasis on God's absolute independence—that He is not harmed by enmity nor strengthened by support—reshape your understanding of sin and repentance? What does it mean for your accountability if God remains untouched by your actions?

First Proof: The Existence of Suffering Disbelievers Disproves al-Aslah
First Sunni proof against al-aslah with illustrative story.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the existence of poor, suffering disbelievers disproves the claim that God must provide the best for everyone.

Core Idea
If al-aslah were obligatory, God would not create suffering disbelievers.

The first Sunni proof against the Mu'tazilite doctrine of al-aslah is straightforward: if God had to do what is most advantageous for everyone, He would not create disbelievers who suffer in both worlds. An anecdote about al-Hafiz Ibn Hajar illustrates that worldly conditions are relative to the hereafter.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof and story
Read the Arabic passage and translation to understand the proof.
2
Analyze the logical structure
The proof is a conditional: if al-aslah were obligatory, then God would not create suffering disbelievers. Since He does create them, al-aslah is not obligatory.
This is a modus tollens argument.
3
Understand the story
The story of Ibn Hajar and the Jew illustrates the relativity of worldly misery and blessing compared to the afterlife. The Jew's shabbiness is a paradise compared to hell, while Ibn Hajar's comfort is a prison compared to paradise.
4
Connect story to proof
How does the story support the proof? It shows that God's distribution of worldly conditions is not based on giving everyone the best possible situation, but is relative to the ultimate justice of the afterlife.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلنا: أنَّ الأمر يستلزم الإرادة — إنما يكون ذلك من شأن حكيم محتاج إلى طاعةِ الأولياء أو رجوع الأعداء، يتعزّز بكثرة الأعوان والأنصار، وتعظم لديه الأقدار، لا في حكيم غني كلَّ الغِنَى عن مُوالاةِ الأولياء، قادر كلَّ القدرة على الانتقام من الأعداء، لا تنفعه طاعة من أطاعه، ولا تضره معاداة من عاداه، لا يعتز بكثرة الأنصار والأعوان، ولا تخذله الأعداء ولو كان بعدد القطر والحصى والتراب.

وقد استدلَّ أهل السنة والجماعة لصحة ما ذهبوا إليه من أنَّه لا يجب على الله تعالى فعل الصَّلاح والأصلح لأحدٍ من عباده بوجوه: الوجه الأول: أنه لو وجب عليه الأصلح لعباده لما خلق الكافِرَ الفقير المُعذَّبَ في الدُّنيا والآخرة، سيّما المبتلى بالأسقام والآلام والمِحَنِ والآفات. وقد حُكِي أن الحافظ ابن حجر مرَّ يوماً بالسُّوق في موكب عظيم وهَيَّاةٍ جَمِيلةٍ، فهجَمَ عليه يهودي وأثوابه مُلَطَّخةٌ بالدَّرَنِ، وهو في غاية الرَّثَاثة والبشاعة، فقَبَضَ على لِجَامِ بغلته، ثم قال له: يا شيخ الإسلام، أنت تزعم أن نبيكم قال: «الدنيا سجن المؤمن، وجنة الكافر»، فأي سجن هذا أنتَ فيه مع النعمة؟ وأي جنَّة أنا فيها مع ما ترى؟ فقال له الحافظ: أما أنا، فإنَّ الذي أنا فيه بالنسبة لما أعده الله في الآخرة مِن النّعيم للمؤمنين يُعدُّ سجناً، وأما أنت، فإن الذي أنت فيه بالنسبة لما ينتظرك من العذاب الأليم يُعَدُّ جَنَّةٌ.
We said: The command entails the will — that is only the concern of a wise person who is in need of the obedience of friends or the return of enemies, who gains strength through many supporters and helpers, and for whom positions become great. Not so for a Wise Being who is absolutely independent of the friendship of friends, absolutely able to take vengeance on enemies; the obedience of those who obey Him does not benefit Him, nor does the enmity of those who oppose Him harm Him; He is not strengthened by many helpers and supporters, nor do enemies weaken Him even if they were as numerous as the drops of rain, pebbles, and dust.

The People of the Sunnah and the Community have adduced proofs for the correctness of their position that it is not obligatory upon God Almighty to do what is proper or most proper for any of His servants in several ways: The first proof: that if the most proper were obligatory upon Him for His servants, He would not have created the poor disbeliever who is tormented in this world and the Hereafter, especially one afflicted with sicknesses, pains, trials, and afflictions. It has been related that al-Hafiz Ibn Hajar one day passed through the market in a great procession and a fine appearance. A Jew rushed upon him, his garments stained with filth, in the utmost shabbiness and ugliness. He grabbed the bridle of his mule and said to him: 'O Sheikh of Islam, you claim that your Prophet said: "The world is a prison for the believer and a paradise for the disbeliever." So what kind of prison is this that you are in, with blessing? And what kind of paradise am I in, with what you see?' Al-Hafiz said to him: 'As for me, what I am in, compared to what God has prepared in the Hereafter of bliss for the believers, is considered a prison. As for you, what you are in, compared to what awaits you of painful punishment, is considered a paradise.'

First, there is a quick response to the Mu'tazilite analogy: the command to act kindly applies only to a wise person who is dependent on others; God is absolutely independent, so the analogy fails. Then the author introduces three proofs. The first proof is presented: if al-aslah were obligatory, God would not create a poor disbeliever who suffers in both worlds. The story of al-Hafiz Ibn Hajar illustrates this: a Jew in misery challenges the hadith that the world is a prison for the believer and a paradise for the disbeliever. Ibn Hajar replies that relative to the hereafter, the believer's worldly blessings are still a prison compared to paradise, and the disbeliever's worldly misery is a paradise compared to hellfire. Thus, the existence of suffering disbelievers shows that God is not obligated to give everyone the most advantageous situation; His wisdom and justice are not bound by human notions of al-aslah.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Worldly misery of disbelievers is a sign of God's injustice or that He is not obligated to be just.
Correction: Worldly misery is relative; God's justice is seen in the afterlife, and He is not obligated to give everyone the best worldly situation.

C) God always does what is best

The existence of suffering disbelievers proves that God is not obligated to provide what is most advantageous to everyone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a poor disbeliever suffering, do you truly believe that God's wisdom is greater than your sense of fairness? How does this passage challenge your instinct to judge God's actions?
?
Ibn Hajar's reply to the Jew was immediate and certain. Do you have that same certainty about the afterlife? If the Jew had asked you, would you have been able to answer with such conviction? What does that reveal about your faith?
?
This proof shows that God is not obligated to give everyone what is best for them in this world. How does that affect the way you view your own hardships? Are you willing to accept that your suffering may be a prison compared to Paradise, or do you secretly resent God for not giving you an easier life?

The Fifth Objection: A Rational Dilemma Against al-aslah
Sanusi presents the fifth objection against the doctrine of al-aslah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the fifth objection exposes the logical inconsistency in the Mu'tazilite doctrine that God must do what is most beneficial for His servants.

Core Idea
If God must do what is best, He would kill potential sinners as children.

Sanusi presents a powerful logical argument against the Mu'tazilite claim that God is obliged to do what is most beneficial (al-aslah). He shows that if al-aslah were true, God would have to prevent all possibility of sin, which He does not. This objection prepares the ground for the famous story of the three brothers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the logical structure: premise, dilemma, objection, response, counter.
Pay attention to the conditional phrases.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite premise
The passage begins by stating the conclusion that follows from the Mu'tazilite doctrine: God did not do what was obligatory. Find the premise that leads to this.
The premise is that God must do what is most beneficial (al-aslah).
3
Follow the dilemma
Sanusi then presents two scenarios: either the beneficial is taklif or it is not. See how each leads to a contradiction.
4
Examine the counter-argument
The Mu'tazila might respond that God knew some people would cause harm, so He killed them for their benefit. Sanusi counters by asking why notorious sinners were not killed as children.
This highlights the inconsistency.
5
Reflect on the force of the objection
Consider how this argument undermines the entire Mu'tazilite position on divine obligation.
This is a turning point in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الخامس : أنه يلزم على مذهبهم أن الله تعالى لم يفعل ما وجب عليه مع كلِّ مِمَّن عاش سليما حتى بلغ وكفر أو ارتد بعد الإسلام، لأنَّ إماتته في حال الصبي أو سَلْبَ عقله كان أصلح له، ولم يفعله ، فإن زعمتم أنَّ الأصلح هو التكليف والتعريض للنعيم المقيم لكون ذلك أعلى المَنْزِلَتَيْنِ، وقد فعله هؤلاء الذين ذكرتم، قلنا: فلا يكون قد فعل ما هو الأصلح بكلِّ مَن أماته في حال الصبى أو أورثه الجنونَ ؛ لأنَّه لم يبق واحدا من هؤلاء حتى يبلغ سليما فيعرضه لأعلى المنزلتين. فإن قيل : عَلِم مِن هؤلاء أنَّ واحدا منهم لو عاش لضلّ وأَضَلَّ غيره فأماته لمصلحته ومصلحة غيره ، قلنا : فلِمَ لم يُمِتْ «فِرعَوْنَ» و«هامان» «ومَزْدَكَ» وزَرَادِشْتَ» وغيرهم مِن الضَّالِّين والمضلين أطفالاً ؛ وهذا الوجه هو لباب المناقشة التي دارت بين أبي هاشم الجُبَّائي وأبي الحسن الأشعري ، وكان الأشعري أحد تلامذة الجبائي ، وكان الشيخ يُقرِّرُ في درسه يوما هذه المسألة ، فقال أبو الحسن: ما تقول في ثلاثة إخوة مات أحدهم كبيرًا طائعاً ، ومات الثاني كبيرًا عاصياً ، ومات الثالث صغيرًا ؟ فقال الجبائي : الأول يُثاب بالجنة ، والثاني يُثاب بالنار ، والثالث لا يثاب ولا يعاقب ؛ (وذلك على قولهم بالمنزلة بين المنزلتين). فقال الأشعري: فإن قال الثالث : يا ربِّ ، لأيِّ شَيْءٍ أَمَتَنِي صغيرًا ولم تُبقنِي حتى أبلغ فأطيعك فأدخل الجنَّةَ ؟ فقال الجبائي: يقول له ربُّه: علمتُ أنك لو كبرت عصيت فتدخل النَّار، فكانَ الأصلح لك أن تموت صغيرًا. فقال الأشعري: فإن قال الثَّاني: يا ربِّ لَمَّا عَلِمْتَ أَنِّي إنْ كبرتُ عصيتُ فدخلتُ النَّارَ ، فلِمَ لم تمتني صغيرًا حَتَّى أكون كأخي ؟ ماذا يقول الرَّبُّ ؟ فَبُهِتَ الجبائي . تُمِثْنِي ومن ذلك الحين ترك الأشعري دروسه ومذهبه واشتغل هو وأتباعه بإبطال مذهب=
The fifth aspect: that from their doctrine it follows that God Most High did not do what was obligatory upon Him with everyone who lived safely until reaching puberty and then disbelieved or apostatized after Islam, because causing his death in childhood or taking away his intellect would have been more beneficial (aslah) for him, yet He did not do that. If you claim that the beneficial (al-aslah) is [actually] the imposition of obligation (taklif) and exposure to eternal bliss because that is the higher of the two stations, and He did that to those you mentioned, we say: then He would not have done what is most beneficial for everyone whom He caused to die in childhood or afflicted with insanity, because not one of those was left to reach maturity safely and be exposed to the higher station. If it is said: He knew of these people that if any of them lived he would go astray and lead others astray, so He caused his death for his welfare and the welfare of others, we say: then why did He not kill Pharaoh, Haman, Mazdak, Zoroaster, and other astray misleaders in childhood? This aspect is the core of the discussion that took place between Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i and Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari. Al-Ash'ari was one of al-Jubba'i's students, and the shaykh was teaching this topic one day in his class. Abu al-Hasan said: 'What do you say about three brothers: one died as an adult obedient, the second died as an adult disobedient, and the third died as a child?' Al-Jubba'i said: 'The first is rewarded with Paradise, the second is punished with Hellfire, and the third receives neither reward nor punishment' (this is based on their doctrine of the intermediate position (al-manzila bayn al-manzilatayn). Al-Ash'ari said: 'If the third says: O Lord, why did you cause me to die as a child and not keep me alive until I could reach maturity and obey You so that I could enter Paradise?' Al-Jubba'i said: 'His Lord would tell him: I knew that if you grew up you would disobey and enter Hellfire, so it was more beneficial for you to die as a child.' Al-Ash'ari said: 'Then if the second says: O Lord, since You knew that if I grew up I would disobey and enter Hellfire, why did You not cause me to die as a child so that I would be like my brother? What would the Lord say?' Then al-Jubba'i was silenced. ... And from that moment al-Ash'ari left his lessons and his doctrine, and he and his followers occupied themselves with refuting the Mu'tazila doctrine...
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage contains the fifth objection (al-wajh al-khamis) against the Mu'tazilite doctrine of al-aslah, followed by the famous story of the three brothers. The argument proceeds as follows: The Mu'tazila claim that God must do what is most beneficial for His servants. But then why does God let certain people reach maturity only to disbelieve or apostatize? Killing them as children would have been more beneficial. If the Mu'tazila respond that the true benefit is the taklif (religious obligation) that leads to eternal bliss, then God has not done what is most beneficial for those He killed as children or made insane, because they were deprived of that opportunity. If the Mu'tazila counter that God knew they would cause harm if they lived, so He killed them for their benefit, then the objection is: why did God not kill all notorious tyrants and misleaders like Pharaoh, Haman, Mazdak, and Zoroaster as children? This exposes the inconsistency. The author then narrates the well-known debate between Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i, the Mu'tazilite scholar, and his student Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari. Al-Ash'ari posed the case of three brothers: one died obedient as an adult, one died disobedient as an adult, and one died as a child. According to Mu'tazilite principles, the child is in an intermediate state (al-manzila bayn al-manzilatayn) — neither rewarded nor punished. Al-Ash'ari then raised a dilemma: the child could ask why he was not kept alive to obey and enter Paradise. Al-Jubba'i's answer (that God knew he would sin) is then turned back against him when al-Ash'ari asks about the disobedient brother: if God knew he would sin, why not kill him as a child to save him from hell? Al-Jubba'i was unable to answer. This debate is said to be the turning point where al-Ash'ari abandoned Mu'tazilism and began refuting it.

Key Terms
الوجه الخامس
الأصلح
التكليف
التعريض للنعيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Mu'tazilite doctrine of al-aslah is obviously true and that God must always do what is best for each individual.
Correction: This passage shows that the doctrine leads to logical absurdities and contradicts observable reality, where God allows many to disbelieve.

Checkpoint
According to the fifth objection, what does the Mu'tazilite claim imply about potential disbelievers?
A) They should be killed as children.
B) They should be given every chance to believe.
C) God does nothing beneficial for them.
D) God is not obligated to do anything.

Key Takeaway
The fifth objection demonstrates that the doctrine of al-aslah leads to absurd consequences and cannot be consistently maintained.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the Mu'tazilite idea that God must do what is best for every individual, how does that challenge your own assumptions about why God allows trials and hardships in your life? Are you willing to trust that His wisdom is deeper than your understanding of 'best'?
?
Sanusi points out that if God were to kill potential sinners as children, He would have killed Pharaoh and others. How does this make you confront the reality that God's plan includes allowing evil people to exist? In what way does this call you to examine your own judgments about who should or should not be given life?
?
This argument shows that the doctrine of al-aslah leads to absurdities. Reflect on your own beliefs: do you ever think you know better than God what is good for you? How can you surrender that attitude and accept divine decree even when it seems less than optimal?

The Story of the Three Brothers: The Debate That Changed al-Ash'ari
Sanusi narrates the debate between al-Ash'ari and al-Jubba'i over the three brothers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the parable of the three brothers exposed the fatal flaw in Mu'tazilite theology and led to al-Ash'ari's conversion.

Core Idea
The dilemma of the three brothers shattered Mu'tazilite coherence.

After establishing the rational objection, Sanusi narrates the famous debate between Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i and his student Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari. This story is not just historical; it illustrates the logical failure of the Mu'tazilite position and marks the birth of Ash'ari theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the story carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to understand the scenario of three brothers and their respective fates. Then read the translation.
Note that the story is a concrete illustration of the abstract objection.
2
Understand al-Jubba'i's answer
Al-Jubba'i gives the fate of each brother according to Mu'tazilite principles: the pious believer to Paradise, the sinful to Hell, and the child to an intermediate state.
This reflects their doctrine of al-manzila bayn al-manzilatayn.
3
Follow al-Ash'ari's first question
Al-Ash'ari asks why the child was not kept alive to obey and enter Paradise. Al-Jubba'i answers by citing God's foreknowledge that the child would sin if he lived.
4
See the turning point: the second question
Al-Ash'ari then asks about the disobedient brother: if God knew he would sin, why was he not killed as a child? Al-Jubba'i is silenced.
This is the moment that exposed the inconsistency.
5
Reflect on the significance
This story is not just a clever argument; it caused al-Ash'ari to abandon Mu'tazilism and start his own school.
It shows the power of logical reasoning in theology.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الخامس : أنه يلزم على مذهبهم أن الله تعالى لم يفعل ما وجب عليه مع كلِّ مِمَّن عاش سليما حتى بلغ وكفر أو ارتد بعد الإسلام، لأنَّ إماتته في حال الصبي أو سَلْبَ عقله كان أصلح له، ولم يفعله ، فإن زعمتم أنَّ الأصلح هو التكليف والتعريض للنعيم المقيم لكون ذلك أعلى المَنْزِلَتَيْنِ، وقد فعله هؤلاء الذين ذكرتم، قلنا: فلا يكون قد فعل ما هو الأصلح بكلِّ مَن أماته في حال الصبى أو أورثه الجنونَ ؛ لأنَّه لم يبق واحدا من هؤلاء حتى يبلغ سليما فيعرضه لأعلى المنزلتين. فإن قيل : عَلِم مِن هؤلاء أنَّ واحدا منهم لو عاش لضلّ وأَضَلَّ غيره فأماته لمصلحته ومصلحة غيره ، قلنا : فلِمَ لم يُمِتْ «فِرعَوْنَ» و«هامان» «ومَزْدَكَ» وزَرَادِشْتَ» وغيرهم مِن الضَّالِّين والمضلين أطفالاً ؛ وهذا الوجه هو لباب المناقشة التي دارت بين أبي هاشم الجُبَّائي وأبي الحسن الأشعري ، وكان الأشعري أحد تلامذة الجبائي ، وكان الشيخ يُقرِّرُ في درسه يوما هذه المسألة ، فقال أبو الحسن: ما تقول في ثلاثة إخوة مات أحدهم كبيرًا طائعاً ، ومات الثاني كبيرًا عاصياً ، ومات الثالث صغيرًا ؟ فقال الجبائي : الأول يُثاب بالجنة ، والثاني يُثاب بالنار ، والثالث لا يثاب ولا يعاقب ؛ (وذلك على قولهم بالمنزلة بين المنزلتين). فقال الأشعري: فإن قال الثالث : يا ربِّ ، لأيِّ شَيْءٍ أَمَتَنِي صغيرًا ولم تُبقنِي حتى أبلغ فأطيعك فأدخل الجنَّةَ ؟ فقال الجبائي: يقول له ربُّه: علمتُ أنك لو كبرت عصيت فتدخل النَّار، فكانَ الأصلح لك أن تموت صغيرًا. فقال الأشعري: فإن قال الثَّاني: يا ربِّ لَمَّا عَلِمْتَ أَنِّي إنْ كبرتُ عصيتُ فدخلتُ النَّارَ ، فلِمَ لم تمتني صغيرًا حَتَّى أكون كأخي ؟ ماذا يقول الرَّبُّ ؟ فَبُهِتَ الجبائي . تُمِثْنِي ومن ذلك الحين ترك الأشعري دروسه ومذهبه واشتغل هو وأتباعه بإبطال مذهب=
The fifth aspect: that from their doctrine it follows that God Most High did not do what was obligatory upon Him with everyone who lived safely until reaching puberty and then disbelieved or apostatized after Islam, because causing his death in childhood or taking away his intellect would have been more beneficial (aslah) for him, yet He did not do that. If you claim that the beneficial (al-aslah) is [actually] the imposition of obligation (taklif) and exposure to eternal bliss because that is the higher of the two stations, and He did that to those you mentioned, we say: then He would not have done what is most beneficial for everyone whom He caused to die in childhood or afflicted with insanity, because not one of those was left to reach maturity safely and be exposed to the higher station. If it is said: He knew of these people that if any of them lived he would go astray and lead others astray, so He caused his death for his welfare and the welfare of others, we say: then why did He not kill Pharaoh, Haman, Mazdak, Zoroaster, and other astray misleaders in childhood? This aspect is the core of the discussion that took place between Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i and Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari. Al-Ash'ari was one of al-Jubba'i's students, and the shaykh was teaching this topic one day in his class. Abu al-Hasan said: 'What do you say about three brothers: one died as an adult obedient, the second died as an adult disobedient, and the third died as a child?' Al-Jubba'i said: 'The first is rewarded with Paradise, the second is punished with Hellfire, and the third receives neither reward nor punishment' (this is based on their doctrine of the intermediate position (al-manzila bayn al-manzilatayn). Al-Ash'ari said: 'If the third says: O Lord, why did you cause me to die as a child and not keep me alive until I could reach maturity and obey You so that I could enter Paradise?' Al-Jubba'i said: 'His Lord would tell him: I knew that if you grew up you would disobey and enter Hellfire, so it was more beneficial for you to die as a child.' Al-Ash'ari said: 'Then if the second says: O Lord, since You knew that if I grew up I would disobey and enter Hellfire, why did You not cause me to die as a child so that I would be like my brother? What would the Lord say?' Then al-Jubba'i was silenced. ... And from that moment al-Ash'ari left his lessons and his doctrine, and he and his followers occupied themselves with refuting the Mu'tazila doctrine...
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage contains the fifth objection (al-wajh al-khamis) against the Mu'tazilite doctrine of al-aslah, followed by the famous story of the three brothers. The argument proceeds as follows: The Mu'tazila claim that God must do what is most beneficial for His servants. But then why does God let certain people reach maturity only to disbelieve or apostatize? Killing them as children would have been more beneficial. If the Mu'tazila respond that the true benefit is the taklif (religious obligation) that leads to eternal bliss, then God has not done what is most beneficial for those He killed as children or made insane, because they were deprived of that opportunity. If the Mu'tazila counter that God knew they would cause harm if they lived, so He killed them for their benefit, then the objection is: why did God not kill all notorious tyrants and misleaders like Pharaoh, Haman, Mazdak, and Zoroaster as children? This exposes the inconsistency. The author then narrates the well-known debate between Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i, the Mu'tazilite scholar, and his student Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari. Al-Ash'ari posed the case of three brothers: one died obedient as an adult, one died disobedient as an adult, and one died as a child. According to Mu'tazilite principles, the child is in an intermediate state (al-manzila bayn al-manzilatayn) — neither rewarded nor punished. Al-Ash'ari then raised a dilemma: the child could ask why he was not kept alive to obey and enter Paradise. Al-Jubba'i's answer (that God knew he would sin) is then turned back against him when al-Ash'ari asks about the disobedient brother: if God knew he would sin, why not kill him as a child to save him from hell? Al-Jubba'i was unable to answer. This debate is said to be the turning point where al-Ash'ari abandoned Mu'tazilism and began refuting it.

كبير عاص
صغير
المنزلة بين المنزلتين
بهت
الجُبَّائي
الأشعري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think al-Ash'ari rejected reason and relied solely on revelation.
Correction: This story shows al-Ash'ari using logical reasoning to refute the Mu'tazilite position; reason is central to his later theology.

Checkpoint
Why was al-Jubba'i silenced in the debate?
A) He could not explain why the disobedient brother was not killed as a child if God knew he would sin.
B) He admitted that the child should be punished.
C) Al-Ash'ari brought a revelation from the Quran.
D) The child could not be saved.

Key Takeaway
The story of the three brothers shows that the Mu'tazilite principle of al-aslah is self-contradictory when applied consistently.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief so tightly that you refused to consider a contradictory scenario? What would it take for you to abandon a deeply held conviction about God?
?
When you think about the third brother who died young, do you ever feel that your own circumstances in life are 'unfair'? How does this story challenge your trust in God's wisdom over your own sense of fairness?
?
Al-Ash'ari walked away from his teacher and his entire worldview after this debate. Is there a belief or habit in your life that you know is inconsistent, yet you cling to it because change is too costly? What might you need to leave behind to be truthful to what you know?
